THE TWO CITIES.
On the dusky s re of evening, stretched in
	shining peace it lies,
City built of clouds and sunshine—wonder of
	the Western skies.
While I watch and long for pinions, thither-
	ward to take my flight,
Slow the ærial city fadeth, and vanisheth from
	sight.
Ruby dome and silver temple, circling wall of
	amethyst,
Fall in silence, leaving only purple ruin hung
	with mist.
Darkness gathers, Eastward, Westward; strong-
	er waxeth my desire,
Reaching through celestial spaces, glittering
	as with rain of fire.
To the city set with jasper, having twelve
	foundations fair,
Flashing from their jeweled splendor every col-
	or soft and rare.
Twelve in number are its gateways—numbered
	by the seer of old—
Every gate a pearl most lustrous—and its
	streets are paved with gold.
In the midst, in dazzling whiteness, lightens
	the eternal throne;
From it flows the living water—round it gleams
	an emerald zone.
Luscious fruits and balmy odors, healing leaves
	and cooling shade,
Either side the life tree sheddeth, by sweet
	storms of music swayed.
O, thou grand, untempled city, seen by John
	in vision bright,
Glory-flooded, needing neither sun by day nor
	moon by night.
Filled forever and forever by the shining
	light of Him
Who redeemed the world, and sitteth throned
	between the Seraphlm!
Through thy lovely gates the nations of the
	saved in triumph stream,
Chanting praise above all praises—love of
	love their holy theme!
They no more shall thirst or hunger—they no
	more with heat shall faint;
Christ for tears will give them gladness—bliss-
	ful rest for sore complaint.
“Blessed they who do His bidding!" cries the
	angel, day and night;
They shall find abundant entrance—they shall
	walk with Him in white!
SELECTED.


MEMORIAL SERVICES AT MOUNT VERNON.
	The one hundredth anniversary of the
death of George Washington, which occured
December 14, 1799, was appropriately re-
membered by the patriotic and Masonic
societies throughout the land.
	At Mount Vernon the scene was impres-
sive as conducted by the Masonic Order.
President McKinley, accompanied by mem-
bers of his Cabinet, attended the exercises
and delivered an address. The procession
that moved up the slope to the mansion
consisted of the Third United States
Cavalry band, Grand Tiler of the Grand
Lodge of Virginia, Master Masons of the
District of Columbia, visiting Master
Masons and Local Lodges.
	President McKinley reviewed the pro-
cession with uncovered head, and, as the
last of it passed the mansion, the presiden-
tial party fell in line at the rear and marched
to the tomb where Washington was first in-
terred, and where Masonic exercises were
held one hundred years ago.
	A dirge by the Marine band was played
and a prayer was offered by the Right Rev.
A. M. Randolph, Bishop of the Episcopal
diocese of Southern Virginia. The Grand
Master Mason of Colorado then delivered an
address.
	At the conclusion of the speech the pro-
cession was formed with the Grand Master
of Virginia, Grand officers and invited
guests, the Grand Masters of the thirteen
original states, together with the three
lodges, and proceeded to the present tomb.
Arriving there, the Grand Master of Vir-
ginia and other Grand officers took a posi-
tion in front of the tomb. The ceremonies
consisted of a prayer by the Grand Chaplain
and a speech by the Grand Master of Vir-
ginia. He asked for responses and greet-
ings from the thirteen original states, and
these were given by their respective Grand
Masters.
	The ritual was read and the Grand
Master deposited evergreen wreaths upon
the tomb. Beginning with sunrise and
ending with sunset a gun was fired every
half hour at Fort Washington. A platoon
of Light Battery, M. Seventh United States
Artillery from Washington Barracks, fired a
salute of twenty-one guns, and at the con-
clusion of the ceremonies at the new tomb
three volleys were fired by a detachment of
Battery A, Fourth United States Artillery,
Fort Hunt, Va. When the President fin-
shed his address twenty-one guns were
fired by the United States steamship, Sylph[-]
While the guns from the war vessel were
booming, the entire assembly sang
"America.”
	The Masons had scarcely departed from
Mount Vernon when the Red Men arrived
at the tomb. They went through the cere-
monies prepared by the committee appointed
at the last session of the Great Council of
the United States, held in Washington
last September. The ceremony was per-
formed in the evening at their home lodges
by 2,200 different tribes throughout the
country.
Spirit of '76.


MISS ANTHONY'S LETTER.
ROCHESTER, N. Y., Dec. 25, 1899.
	In this, my eightieth year, I am filled
with a great desire to urge all believers in
the political enfranchisement of women to
manifest that belief in some material way.
Will you not, as a New Year’s pledge,
promised to aid the Suffrage Association in
some direct manner? No woman is so
situated that she cannot do something.
Write me, therefore, that you will do one or
all of the following things: Become a
member of our local club or State Associa-
tion, organize a club, write to the president
of your State Association, telling her that
you stand ready and willing to do anything
in your power which she may call upon
you to do, or contribute a certain amount of
money to the treasury of your local county,
state or national society, that your name
may be counted among those who love the
cause of equal rights for women.
	The command to labor for the elevation
of human kind is not upon a chosen few
only, but upon every intelligent being. I
send this appeal to you personally, and I
beg you to reply to me personally.
Yours sincerely,
SUSAN B. ANTHONY.


ANNUAL CONVENTION N. A. W. S. A.
	The thirty-second annual convention of
the National Woman’s Suffrage Associa-
tion will be held in Washington, in the
Church of our Father, February 8 to 14,
inclusive.
	The nineteenth century was called by
the poet prophet, Victor Hugo, “The
Century of Woman.” Since the dawn of
history exceptional women have demon-
strated by their high abilities as inventors,
skilled workers, poets, artists, teachers and
rulers, that in the domain of intellect woman
was not under the ban of Nature, but under
that of statute and custom. But for the
mass of women the nineteenth century has
been the only epoch in history which has
given them rights as women or opportuni-
ties as human beings.
	To state the difference in the position of
women in all fields of activity between
what it was at the beginning of the century
and is now at its close, would be to present
a series of transformations surpassing the
magic of Aladdin’s lamp.
	The way up the height of woman’s ad-
vancement has been long and steep, but it
has not been dreary. The consciousness of
giving the world a forward movement along
the path of liberty is the highest reward
vouchsafed to human effort. The greatest
men of the century have walked with us.
Poets have sung for us; prophets have
inspired us with visions of success; states-
men have made courts and forums ring
with eloquence in our behalf. Brickbats
have blossomed into roses; scorn has be-
come applause; timidity, opposition and
indifference have changed into a grand
chorus of appeal for woman’s equality be-
fore the law.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


Let us then close the century with a con-
vention which shall be a jubilee for our
successes and preparation for the twentieth
century, which is to be not man’s nor wom-
an’s, but humanity’s.
	SUSAN B. ANTHONY,
President.
	ANNA H. SHAW.
Vice-President.
	RACHEL FOSTER AVERY,
Corresponding Secretary.
	ALICE STONE BLACKWELL,
Recording Secretary.

LADIES’ SEMI-MONTHLY MEETING.
	The Semi-Monthly Meeting was held
November 25, 1899, at the usual hour and
place, Counselor E. J. Stevenson presiding.
	Meeting opened by singing “O awake,
my slumbering minstrel.” Prayer by
Counselor Elizabeth Webb. Singing,
“Now let us rejoice.” Minutes of the
previous meeting read and approved.
	Sister Stevenson said Sister Zina D. H.
Young and herself had visited Cache Valley
last week. They had excellent meetings
in numbers, and also a great flow of the
spirit.
	President M. I. Horne hoped all had
come today to receive a blessing and had
left their cares at home. Felt we were pros-
pering temporally and spiritually. God
has chosen us out of the world, and we
should all examine ourselves and see if we
are living the life of a Saint. The Lord
has given to his children in the world great
knowledge, but they have not had given to
them the privilege of the Gospel. Spoke of
the wonderful inventions and discoveries
since the organization of the Church, and
we have the benefit of them. The Saints
could not be brought to this land so safely
without the modern improvements. Our
enemies can take everything of a temporal
nature from us, but they cannot take the
testimony of the Gospel. God is no re-
spector of persons. He has bestowed upon
those in the world much useful knowledge,
but to us He has given higher duties, Tem-
ple work, etc.
	Dr. E. S. Barney spoke of the faithful-
ness of President Horne and thought we
should hold up her hands as those of Moses
were held up. Said: “She has been guided
by inspiration, her works speak for her. As
you labor God will reward you.”
	Sister Jane James and Sister Butterworth
each made good remarks.
	Sister Lula Greene Richards spoke of the
old Retrenchment Association and the neces-
sity of reviving these things again. Spoke
of the covenant she had entered into with
many other sisters, in the Tabernacle, by
request of President Young, that she would
wear plain hats. And she knew if we
would be more plainly dressed, retrench,
not follow the fashions of Babylon, we
would be more blessed.
	Sister Almeda Young thought we should
not follow all the fashions of the world and
wished to be one who would dress plainly,
for she knew it to be more pleasing to the
Lord.
	Sister Julia C. Howe believed the same

[Column 2]
spirit that rested upon President Snow in
regard to paying tithing, rests upon Sister
Horne in reviving retrenchment. Thought
we should retrench in eating and drinking,
dressing, etc.
	Sister Zina D. H Young said: “We are
blessed with the spirit of the living God
here today. Anything we ask for of the
Lord in righteousness will be given us.
Every face here is like a star.” Said we
had a right to inspiration to help us in our
work. Spoke of the good example Presi-
dent Young had set for the people and
these organizations, and how they had been
blessed in the work.
	Sister Martha Morris rejoiced that she
was present. Said let us be one, love one
another and be true to our Maker.
	Sister Maria W. Wilcox said she knew
this was the true Church. Knew we would
be rewarded if we would keep the com
mandments of God. Thought the spirit of
retrenchment should be brought among us.
	Sister Julia S. Woolley believed it was
necessary to teach our children economy
and retrenchment, for the punishments
promised would be fulfilled.
	Sister Annie T. Hyde felt we should re-
trench, had felt it for a great while. Said
our daughters should not follow the fash-
ions. Announced that two weeks from
that day a lecture would be given in the
Templeton, at 3 o’clock, the first of a series
on hygiene, anatomy, physiology, wife-
hood, motherhood, etc., given by Dr.
Margaret C. Roberts and others.
	President Horne said she always wore
straw hats made at home as long as she
could get them. She believed in wearing
home made clothing.
	Meeting adjourned for two weeks. Sing-
ing, “Come let us anew.” Benediction by
Sister Clara C. Cannon.

	Minutes of the meeting held January 6,
1900, Sister E. J. Stevenson presiding.
	Meeting opened with singing, and prayer
by Counselor E. Webb. Singing, “Come
let us anew.” Minutes of previous meeting
read by Sister A. T. Hyde, the secretary
being absent from home.
	Sister E. J. Stevenson spoke of beginning
the year aright, Said, “Do not speak evil
of one another, let us try to improve our-
selves in this respect.” Spoke of the faith-
fulness of Mrs. M. A. Hyde, who was
present and would speak to us. Spoke of
teaching our children the need of retrench-
ment, never run in debt and impress our
children never to. Spoke of the death of
F. D. Richards, the friend of woman. Said
to be exemplary mothers and missionaries,
for there is great need of missionary work
here as well as abroad.
	Sister Eliza Angel felt that we had had a
glorious meeting at our confe[-]ence. She
had been in the Church forty-five years.
	Sister Wright, Sister Sarah Phelps and
another sister (name unknown) spoke upon
interesting subjects, and a sister sang a
hymn.
	Sister M. A. Hyde never came here but
she felt the sweet spirit that was manifest
at these meetings. In the year 1843 she
went to an evening meeting and spoke in
tongues, although it was her first attempt
at speaking. She had the privilege of
hearing the Prophet Joseph when he said
that things would transpire in the last days
that would make our ears tingle. She felt
to put her trust in the Lord. Not many
have too much wealth, we are blessed if we
have sufficient for the day. Said it was the

[Column 3]
gift of her husband, Apostle Orson Hyde,
to understand the signs of the times, and
he often spoke of them. She felt as if she
were left alone, for so many had passed
away. Loved to commune with the Saints
and said, “Let us build each other up, for
there is no other joy than to serve the
Lord.”
	Sister Hardy hoped to be able to stand
faithful. Enjoyed the teachings that were
given on the anniversary of the birthday of
the Prophet Joseph Smith.
	A sister in the meeting bore a very
strong and faithful testimony.
	Sister Horne felt that it was here that we
listened to each others’ testimonies, and it
was always a feast. Felt those who were
diligent in the different organizations were
heaven’s nobility. There were tests that
were trying to the aged and also the youth.
Encouraged parents to have their children
attend to their prayers. Said many neither
teach their children nor have family prayers.
Spoke of the union in the 22nd ward branch
and the great good they had accomplished.
Said we must reconcile ourselves to the de-
vision of the Stake.
	Sister Morris sang “Redeemer of
Israel.”
	Sister Webb spoke of the new year. Said
we should cultivate the spirit that comes
from the Lord, which is humility, long-
suffering charity and love. It is necessary
that we be chastened, but He will always
succor us if we put our trust in Him.
Hoped we would always be on the watch-
tower and exhorted all to go to the Temple.
Said how would we feel if the Temple were
closed?
	Another sister felt like acknowledging
the goodness of the Lord. Spoke of the
Temple work, and the beautiful influence
that she felt on her return home after work-
ing at the Temple.
	Sister Brown hoped we would attend to
our Temple work. She heard President
Snow say, that the one who did work for
the dead was a savior to that person, and it
would certainly be recorded to his or her
credit.
	A sister (name unknown) spoke of Tem-
ple work. Said if we worked for the dead
we would be blest.
	Sister Mary Morris said, “It seems just
as good to me to work for my neighbor as
for myself,” referring to Temple work.
	Sister Martha Morris bore her testimony
and Sister Stevenson made a few closing
remarks about the necessity of having the
annual meetings continued.
	The congregation joined in singing the
Doxology, and prayer was offered by Sister
Annie T. Hyde.
ANNIE T. HYDE, Sec. pro tem.


MISS ABBIE EASTMAN, of Phœnix
Ariz., inherited five copper claims three
years ago, and has been working ever since
in California and Arizona to earn the money
required to hold them until they could be
developed and sold. She has always been
obliged to earn her own bread, but with the
sight of a fortune before her she worked
harder than ever. She persevered, and
lately sold one claim for $45,000.
	MRS. ADMIRAL DEWEY startled Wash-
ington society by announcing that women
as well as men would be welcome at her
New Year’s reception. This is the revival of
a custom that was abolished in Cleveland’s
a dministration[-]


WOMAN'S EXPONENT.


I FOLLOW THEE.
I follow thee, although thou canst not hear
My footsteps stealing soft beside thine own;
Though all the day and all the night-time lone,
Groping, I see thee, counting it good cheer
If I may walk beside thee, tho’ unknown,
Till love to love responsive shall be grown
And I may touch thy hand and feel thee near.
I follow thee I let my spirit steal
All palpitant and wistful unto thee;
I ask no more but that I still may feel
Thy wide, sweet presence, moving graciously;
I follow thee, and yearning, patient bide
Till thou shalt turn and see me at thy side.
ETHEL ALLEN MURPHY.


CARRARA.
A ship unloading, busy sea-brown hands
Are lifting blocks of marble, one by one;
Quarried where fair Carrara’s golden sands
And purple hills lie sleeping in the sun.
The workman earned his share of daily bread;
The merchant counted up his gains in gold;
“What unwrought statues there,” the artist said,
“What lines of beauty, rare and manifold!
“What grace and glory from these blocks shall
spring!
What light shall clothe them in a little while!
This shapeless block in beauty blossoming,
Shall breathe high thoughts or wear an angel’s
smile.”
O Lives that in a martyr-army stand,
May God’s sweet message come to you and me.
We are the marble, His the Sculptor-Hand
That fashions us for all eternity.
We only feel the pain His chastenings give;
The sharp incisions only can we see.
And He alone, by whom we move and live,
He sees the hidden glory that shall be.
He sees the glory without spot or stain,
The spiritual beauty all unpriced;
And in His love He sends each stroke of pain
To make us like our dear Lord, Jesus Christ.
O, God of Love, give us calm pitying eyes
And sweet patience. Let us also see
The glory and the grace that underlies
Each shapeless mass that waits a touch from Thee.
ANONYMOUS.


ELIZABETH CADY STANTON.
A great and good Elizabeth
Was Hungary’s royal saint,
For whom rare miracles were wrought,
That pious poets paint.
She fought with nature and her heart,
For many weary years,
Resigning ease and joy and love,
For penances and tears.
Once, from excessive prayers and fasts,
Sorrow and scourging sore,
She was about to faint and sink
Upon her chapel’s floor,
When, lo! the water that she craved.
Was by some power divine,
Changed on her trembling, pallid lips.
To good old Tokay wine.
She prayed and wept herself to death,
This hapless saint, Elizabeth!
Another great Elizabeth,
I sing in greater times,
Who merits greater tributes far,
Than these, my halting rhymes.
Here life a noble poem is
in canto eighty-four—
A record of long warfare waged,
And victories galore—
Not hers her own pure flesh to scourge,
But folly, vice and wrong;
At peace with Nature, loving love,
Home, comfort, mirth and song,
She faints not, though the day seems drear,
And hard, and far the goal,
’Tis we who sink and need the cheer,
The good wine of her soul,
Her earnest, royal soul[-]
Still loving life, not fearing death,
God save our Queen Elizabeth!
GRACE GREENWOOD.
November 12, 1899.


[Column 1]
A WOMAN MISSIONARY.

	[Mrs. Addison Pratt on the Society Islands
1850.]
		(Continued.)
	Today I called the native sisters together
to instruct them in their religious duties.
Ellen acted as interpreter, and succeeded
remarkably well. This she has been able
to do for several months. The sisters spoke
in turns, each expressing a desire that I
might soon have a knowledge of their
language, and speak with my own lips
directly to them. I shall soon make an
attempt to do so, and having their faith
and prayers, shall be certain of success.
	October 6th. Memorable day ! Confer-
ence convenes today in Salt Lake City. The
remembrance creates deep sensations in my
mind. I sigh to be there, although I do
not wish to leave my labors here. Before
another month rolls around “Paraita” will
be gone to Ravavai, then we shall be still
more lonely. How much it would animate
us could we see business going on; see men
plowing, sowing grain, driving teams;
have the sound of axes and hammering;
see houses building. But here monotony
reigns supremely. All I hear is the sound
of mallets making native cloth.
	On the 8th, (Wednesday) a party of us
went on a pleasure excursion to some small
islands five miles distant. We landed on
the largest, nineteen souls in all. It is a
beautiful island covered all over with tama-
na and cocoanut trees. The soil is fine,
white sand, which contrasts beautifully
with the green trees. I walked around the
Island, picked up shells and some fine
specimens of coral. The men caught fish,
made an oven in the ground and cooked us
a meal consisting of fruits and vegetables
growing on the Island. The men threw
up a shelter for us with brush and leaves,
under which we spread our bedding and
slept well through the night. The children
explored the Island thoroughly, climbed the
trees, made new discoveries in shrubbery
and flowers. We sailed home with a
pleasant breeze, and passed the village
where the workmen were engaged building
a schooner. They saluted us with loud
voices, "Iu ora nu otou to have ra mai,"
(peace be to you in returning home.)
	I sometimes walk to the opposite village
where the ship is building; distance five
miles. I can walk under the shade of trees
the whole way, sit down in a cooling shade,
rest myself and renew my jonrney. Thus
we resort to changes, and trifling though
they be, they serve to enliven the mind.
	Mr. Pratt preaches regularly in the
native tongue, which I can now under-
stand tolerably well. Baptisms occur
nearly every Sabbath. I wrote an address
to be read in a woman’s meeting, which Mr.
Grouard translated into the Tahitian for me.
They seemed greatly to admire it. I read
to them the 12th chapter of Isiaah, con-
taining, proof of the origin of the Book of
Mormon. Ellen explained and commented
upon it. They inquired if the ancient
Nephites were Europeans? I told them
they were the ancient fathers of the Tahi-
tians. At this they appeared greatly inter-
ested, and wished to learn more about the
book. No organ of their craniums is more
prominent than marvelousness.
	October 19th. Sabbath it is with us, but
not with those at home. One day is lost in
coming here via Cape Horne, one gained by
coming via Cape Good Hope. The natives
have a superstition that if they should fail

[Column 2]
to keep the right Sabbath someone of their
number would sicken and die. Yesterday
Captain Johnson’s vessel sailed. It has
been lying here several days. We were
hoping it would bring us letters from home,
but only one came to us from Tahiti, writ-
ten by Elder Dunn, who is now nearly
ready to embark for America. He is in
very poor health, and we feel in doubt
about his reaching home, but pray earnest-
ly that he may. So our dear little Mary
must leave us with her step-father, Captain
J.   Seven months has she been a visitant
in our family, and by her modest behavior
has endeared herself to us all, more especial-
ly Frances, who has been her constant
teacher. As she has never been fond of
children, her attachment to Mary, we hope,
may effect a change in her. So submissive
and obedient has she ever been to Frances,
she cannot avoid feeling deep sympathy for
her.
	The 24th. Today arrived a schooner
from the Chain Islands, Captain Hervey.
The king of Ravaivai was on board. He
informed us that the missionary house
built of late has been thrown down in a
gale of wind. Last evening native dancing
was heard, much to our annoyance. Their
music consisted of two drums, a small
instrument made of a weed which grows
here, making a low, sweet sound. Captain
Hervey and Mr. Bourne attended the exer-
cises, drank too freely, quarelled and fought.
Today they had a court over the affray.
Such is the example white men set before
the natives.
	The 27th day. Set sail for Raivavai
with a fine breeze; eighty natives on board.
The air being pleasant, we all slept on
deck. Some of the Island women had
never been so far from home before. They
were very sea sick, although all their lives
accustomed to go out in open boats.
	The second day at ten o’clock we made
the island. It is more mountainous than
Tahiti. The small town near where we
landed presents a lively appearance from
the sea. The house of worship looks like a
city hotel, with white walls and green
window blinds. A long row of ito trees
standing in front, small trunks and large
tops, gives the town the appearance of an
Eastern village at a distance, but as you
draw near the scene changes. All around
the “Fere bare ra” the place is thoroughly
clean. A wide stone pavement surrounds
it. The inside work displays taste and
ingenuity. The pulpit is built very high
and the stairs leading up to it are painted
green. As I entered the building, I thought
how much the poor people had striven to
imitate civilized life. The seats are made
of dark wood the color of mahogany, with
a great variety of carved work upon them.
	Queen Rupah, from Tupuin, came with
ns. She brought her fine clotting given
her by the French merchants. She dressed
very handsomely to go on shore, but would
not wear shoes. The king of the Island
appears a kind, good man, is full in the
faith that his people will receive the Gospel.
Those belonging to the English church
commenced looking in their Bibles to forti-
fy themselves with arguments, should any
attack be made against, their doctrines. The
king built a house, intending to have Paraita
and his family remain there, but its being
destroyed by a wind storm was a premoni-
tion to us that it was not the will of the
Lord that we should make a lengthy stay
there.
		(To be continued.)


Woman's Exponent.
EMMELINE D. WELLS, Editor and Publisher.
Published semi-monthly, in Salt Lake City, Utah,
Terms: one copy one year, $1.00; one copy six months.
50 cts. No reduction made for clubs. City papers deliv-
ered by mail, extra for postage one year, 25 cts.
	Advertising rates: Each square, ten lines of nonpariel
space one time $2.50; per month, $3.00. A liberal dis-
count to regular advertisers.
	EXPONENT office, Room 208 Templeton, South Temple
Street: Business hours from 10 a. m. to 5 p. m. every
day, except Sunday.
	Address all business communications to
		Mrs. E. B. WELLS,

	SALT LAKE CITY, UTAH, JANUARY 1, 1900.


POLYGAMY AND UNLAWFUL
COHABITAION.
	From the reading of the various editorials
and articles of the public press it is evident
that there is much misconstruction and mis-
understanding as to the present attitude of
our Church respecting the subjects of poly-
gamy and unlawful cohabitation; and be-
lieving that many good and conscientious
people have been misled and much adverse
criticism occasioned thereby, I feel it but
just to both “Mormons” and non-“Mor-
mons” to state that, in accordance with the
manifesto of the late President Wilford
Woodruff, dated September 25, 1890,
which was presented and unanimously ac-
cepted by our General Conference on the
6th of October, 1890, the Church has
positively abandoned the practice of poly-
gamy, or the solemnization of plural mar-
riages, in this and every other State, and
that no officer or member thereof has any
authority whatever to perform a plural
marriage or enter into any such relation.
Nor does the Church advise or encourage
unlawful cohabitation on the part of any of
its members. If, therefore, any member
disobeys the law, either as to polygamy or
unlawful cohabitation, he must bear his
own burden: or in other words, be answer-
able to the tribunal of the land for his own
action pertaining thereto.
	With a sincere desire that the position of
our Church as to polygamy and unlawful
cohabitation may be better understood, and
with best wishes for the welfare and happi-
ness of all, this statement is made, and is
respectfully commended to the careful con-
sideration of the public generally.
				LORENZO SNOW.
President of the Church of Jesus Christ of
		Latter-day Saints.
	Salt Lake City, January 8, 1900.


[Column 1]
THE NEW YEAR AND THE NEW
CENTURY.
	In entering upon a new year one always
tries to cast off the old sorrows or troubles,
and look forward with bright anticipation
to the near future, hoping good will come,
prosperity, health and such measure of
happiness as is reasonable to expect.
	America, or the United States at least,
seems in the beginning of this year (said to
be the closing of the nineteenth century,
although there are differences of opinion
among “the wise men” to be in a rathe

[Column 2]
prosperous stage of activity, as compared
with many, indeed most other countries.
So far the people of these United States have
reason to be thankful and to congratulate
themselves, that conditions are so favorable
for future peace and prosperity; yet one
never knows what may transpire. Still it
is right to enjoy the blessings which are
vouchsafed by an Alwise Providence and
trust our Heavenly Father for the future, if
we ourselves comply with His laws and
live by His precepts.
	When one takes into consideration the
calamities that visit the inhabitants of the
earth from time to time, it does seem one
cannot be too careful in keeping all the
commandments God has given to the chil-
dren of men, line upon line and precept
upon precept. The Savior’s injunction,
“Be ye also ready,” is one to be thought of
by all. It is well that time is divided and
subdivided, else when one would stop and
consider anything might be a question in
this day of hurry, worry, bustle and ex-
citement. Holidays are good for the peo-
ple for many reasons. The wish for “a hap-
py new year,” that passes from one to
another, although it is done almost mechan-
cally, is a very good custom. It sets one
to thinking, though the thoughts are often
reminiscent.
	The past year has been one of great sig-
ficance and fraught with much importance
to the world, and also to our own country.
The Peace Conference at The Hague marks
a great change in affairs of the world, al-
though its direct effects are not so tangible
as to satisfy those who looked for im-
mediate arbitration; yet it is a step in the
direction of an era of better things.
	The terrible Transvaal war between
South Africa and Great Britain, is certainly
one of the greatest events in the history of
modern times. The death rate among the
British officers is something appalling. One
would think there was need of peace leagues
and every other kind of organization and
influence, brought to bear in favor of put-
ting a stop to “man’s inhumanity to man.”
Would that the time had come to
	Let those who make the quarrels, be the only
		ones who fight,
as the old song says. One hardly likes to
venture further than generalities in speak-
ing of these matters. That the Lord will
come out of his hiding place and vex the
nations we read in the scriptures, and we
know assuredly that the prophecies of both
ancient and modern prophets will come to
pass; therefore it behooves all in the open-
ing of a new year, as well as at all other
times, to look well to our own conduct, and
not judge others, but leave them in the
hands of the Lord, who will do unto all as
seemeth good in His sight, and who knows
all men’s lives and conditions and the in-
ward promptings of their hearts.
	The past has been a wonderful year for
progress in many lines, and when one con-
templates the century as a whole, the ad-
vancement is greater than any previous
one of which we, who live today, have any
knowledge. In art, in science, (many of .
them) in literature, in inventions, in dis-
coveries, in mental and spiritual develop-
ment and awakening, what marvels have
been wrought!
	The Latter-day Saints as a people have
every reason to rejoice in the achievements
of this glorious century. The Lord has
spoken from the heavens and through his
servants has established a work on the

[Column 3]
earth never to be thrown down, or given to
another people. There is so much to do in
this world of ours, that we have no strength
to waste in repining. We must be active
and watchful and try to help on the world’s
great work, as well as do our individual
own. Selfishness is not the spirit of the
Gospel, but consecration to God and our
fellowmen. To lift up humanity should
be our aim and prayer. Earnestness in
any noble cause will effect much in what-
ever is undertaken with the right motive.
	The women of our people (the Latter-day
Saints) have much to do in rolling on the
world’s great work. Some have already
been set apart to preach the Gospel in
foreign lands, and that many more will be,
there is little doubt. The world is making
great progress, and women are gradually
moving forward into broader fields and be-
coming more and more consecrated in their
lives to the higher spiritual work. When
one calls to mind the work our sisters are
doing in the several woman’s Church or-
ganizations and the Sabbath School, the
ministering in the Temples that have been
built and dedicated to the work of the Lord,
for the living and for the dead, besides the
home teaching, it is grand to contemplate.
Certainly the days are filled up in useful-
ness.
	Whatever may transpire, let the women
of Zion at least be prepared by faith, hope
and trust, and by living daily lives dedi-
cated to the noblest, holiest and purest
motives and impulses, that they may draw
from the fountains of inspiration, wisdom,
knowledge and understanding, and be able
to impart to those less highly, favored, prin-
ciples of truth that shall encourage the
weary and comfort the disconsolate, and
teach those ignorant of divine truths the
way of eternal life, that the daughters of
Zion may have their lamps always trimmed
and burning, not only to light their own
way, but to show others who walk in dark-
ness the true path, if they will but follow
after righteousness.
	The past year has been rich in blessings
for the women of Zion. They have had
great opportunities vouchsafed to them of
enlightenment in many ways, and avenues
have been opened that were scarcely
thought possible—and yet it has all been
in the fulfillment of prophecy, which is a
testimony to the faithful that this is the
work of the Lord, and should cause the
Saints to become mindful of Him and ac-
knowledge His hand in all things, in ad-
versity as well as in prosperity, that He
may not forget them in the hour of trouble
or sorrow. Strive to live by the Golden
Rule and love our neighbor as ourselves,
and try to lift up our fellowmen. One may
easily close one’s exhortation with the
scriptural quotation which covers itr a few
words the present and future:
	Wherefore be ye steadfast, immovable, always
abounding in the work of the Lord, for as much as
ye know that your labor is not in vain in the
Lord.


EDITORIAL NOTES.
	A Happy New Year to all the patrons
and friends of the Woman’s Exponent,
and may it prove a prosperous one to them
and the dear little paper that has been the
champion of the woman’s cause and for
humanity ever since it was established.
May its subscriptions be doubled during
the year Nineteen Hundred.


[Column 1]
	THE Stake Secretaries of the Relief Soci-
ety of the several Stakes of Zion are hereby
reminded that a yearly report is expected of
them for the general report, from the begin-
ning of the year 1899, January 1st to Dec-
ember 31st, and that the report must be
in as early as February 15, so that the
General Secretary may have sufficent time
to make a correct report for the April Con-
ference. All branches of the Relief Society
not included in Stakes are also requested
to send a full report for the year, that a
complete report of the entire society may
be made, including branches, whether
in the United States or other countries, or
upon the islands of the sea. Address Mrs.
E. B. Wells, Secretary, Templeton, Salt
Lake City, Utah.
	THE Seventh Ward Relief Society held
its annual meeting on Thursday, January
4, at 2 p. m. at the commodious home of
Sister Rigby, as it was not convenient to
have the use of the meeting house on that
day, but the rooms were exceedingly com-
fortable. The president, Mary Alice Lam-
bert, stated in her remarks that it was just
thirty-two years that day since the organ-
ization in that ward, and in that time there
had been four presidents. Spoke of the
good work that had been accomplished and
the many ways in which help had been
given. It was a most creditable showing.
It is the intention of the society to have a
house in which to hold meetings, the soci-
ety at present is very prosperous. An ex-
cellent spirit was manifest, and the report
was a very good one. Visitors present
were Sisters E. B. Wells, E. J. Stevenson
and Counselor Annie T. Hyde, of the Stake
Board. President Horne was not able to
come out as the day was inclement. A
sumptuous repast was served at the close of
the meeting, and social conversation, ex-
change of ideas and New Year’s greetings
were general. Such unions help to increase
the interest of the members and are always
pleasant occasions.
	The Woman’s Century Calendar, edited by
Carrie Chapman Catt, and published by N.
A. W. S. A., New York City, is a unique
little book of eighty pages, and contains
much valuable information on the woman
question and excellent photogravures of
some of the prominent women who have
been national leaders along the several
lines of progressive work, Susan B. Anth-
ony, Lucretia Mott, Elizabeth Cady Stan-
ton, Dr. Elizabeth Blackwell, Rev.
Antoinette Brown Blackwell, Lucy Stone,
Frances Willard, Julia Ward Howe, Har-
riet Beecher Stowe, Mary A. Livermore,
Clara Barton and Belle A. Mansfield. The
book is well worth its price, 25 cents, and
every woman who wishes to help
forward the movement for equality, should
purchase a copy. The motto on the cover
is most appropriate, “Not new women, but
new conditions.” The calendar is quite hand-
some, and very suitable to send to those
needing such information on the question
of equal rights and progression.
	Sketches of Missionary Life, is the title of
a very neat little book edited by Edwin F.
Parry, who is able to speak understanding-
iy upon this subject from experience in his
own field of labor. Elder Parry was re-
cently associated in the presidency of the
European mission of the Church of Jesus
Christ of Latter-day Saints, with Rulon S.
Wells as president. The book has one
hundred and forty-four pages and is divided

[Column 2]
into fifteen chapters, treating of the various
phases of an Elder’s life when preaching
the Gospel.—Character of men called, Temp-
tations, Providential aid, First Experiences
in the Field, Miraculous help from the
Lord, Experiences in holding meetings,
Friends raised up—and other equally im-
portant and interesting matters. The book
will undoubtedly be of great service to
young missionaries going forth to the
work of the ministry. The youth of Zion
will find this reading matter of a pure and
elevating character, calculated to inspire
faith in the Latter-day work, evidences of
the divinity of the mission of Joseph Smith,
the prophet. The marvelous way in which
men who trusted in the Lord have had their
prayers answered, the manifestations of
healing and the gift of understanding other
languages and being able to make people
of other tongues understand the Gospel by
the spirit thereof, are all clearly set forth in
this little book. Published and for sale at
George Q. Cannon & Sons Co., Main St.,
Salt Lake City.
	The Englishwoman’s Year Book, for 1900,
edited by Emily Janes, secretary to the
National Union of Women Workers of
Great Britain and Ireland, has been re-
ceived at this office and we are very glad to
acknowledge through our columns so wel-
come a gift, and to recommend it to the
public. The book contains three hundred
and fifty pages and is handsomely bound.
It contains information upon woman’s
work, societies and industries along all lines,
educational, industrial, political, literary,
artistic—whether cooperative, social or
individual. To women visiting Great
Britain such a book would be invaluable,
and to those interested in the work of wom-
en everywhere, it is a storehouse of in-
formation. An item or two in the preface
struck home very forcibly: “The collec-
tive conscience of society, ‘God’s secretary,’
as Milton called it, is awake as it never was
before.” At the close the editor concludes
with an Eastern proverb which she says
may serve as a motto for 1900: “By
patience and perseverance, and a bottle of
sweet oil, the snail at length reaches Jeru-
salem.” The editor deserves great credit
for the able manner in which the year book
is arranged, making it also a directory
wherein one can find the names and ad-
dresses of all the noted women who are en-
gaged in any great work or business, as
well as those who stand officially connected
with institutions. Everyone who rejoices
in the advancement of women must feel
proud of Miss Emily Janes’ success in mak-
ing the book such a valuable authority and
so comprehensive upon subjects of such
great importance to the women of the
world.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.  93


[Column 1]	
		R. S. REPORTS. 

	JOSEPA RELIEF SOCIETY.

   As some of our readers will perhaps re-
member, there was an article in the June
number of the EXPONENT, stating that we
had planted an acre of watermelons with a
view to selling them and using the pro-
ceeds to buy wheat and store up.
   Our melon patch did well and we sold
one hundred and six dollars worth of water-
melons, which you will see will greatly in-
crease our wheat.
   Then we made an experiment in seri-

[Column 2]
culture, raising twenty-three pounds of
cocoons of average quality, and have had
one of our young ladies learn the reeling,
so that we will be better prepared lor the
work another year.
   The Hawaiian sisters are very faithful in
the performance of any trust placed upon
them, and perform their duties in the Re-
lief Society promptly and cheerfully,
whether going out into the field to hoe or
water the melons, or to glean wheat, or
whatsoever they may be doing, sowing or
quilting or waiting on the sick. They al-
ways meet in time to sing a hymn and
then unite in prayer, asking the blessing of
the Lord upon the labor of their hands; and
the Lord does bless them and gives them
success in many ways, and I often think if
we were all more childlike and depended
more on the promised blessings than in our
own wisdom, we would be more happy and
contented.
   I will just mention that we said to the
boys, “I do not think there is one boy in
Josepa so mean that he would steal the
Relief Society melons,’’ and so far as we
know, not one has been stolen. For their
good behavior we gave a watermelon feast
to the whole colony, which was very much
appreciated by old and young.
   We are enjoying our usual good health, 
and a spirit of contentment prevails.
   With “Aloha” to all of our colabors, we
remain.
	Your sisters,
		EMILY G. CLUFF, Pres.,
		ELLEN HALLEMANU, Sec.
		
		WEBER STAKE.

   Minutes of the Relief Society conferenc[e]
of Weber Stake, held in the Third Ward
meeting house, December 14, 1899, at
10 o’clock a. m.
   Meeting was called to order by Counselor
Harriet Brown. After singing, prayer was
offered by Elder Stone. Singing, “Guide
us, O Thou Great Jehovah.” Minutes of
October conference read and approved.
   Sister Brown was pleased to meet with so
many sisters in conference. She knew we
all felt sad over the great bereavement of
our president, and realized that President
Richards would be greatly missed by the
Relief Society of Weber Stake. Prayed
the Lord would bless Sister Richards, that
she might be comforted. Desired all the
sisters to be humble that they might be
blest.
   President C. H. Middleton felt very much
interested in the society and was always 
pleased to meet with the sisters. He spoke
of the great bereavement of Sister Richards,
also of the great loss the death of President
Richards had brought to the Church. Asked
the Lord to bless the sisters, also Sister
Richards, that she might be comforted.
   Brother Woodward, president of the
San Francisco branch, spoke for a few
minutes on the good work of the Relief
Society.
   Testimonies were borne by Sisters C. E.
Driver, Mary Ann Ellis, Rosa Scowcroft, Rachel Burton and Frances Dance.
   Sister E. B. Wells, of Salt Lake City,
said she was very glad to meet with the
sisters. Spoke of her trip to England and
meeting with the sisters of the Relief Soci-
ety there. She deeply sympathized with
Sister Richards, and asked the Lord to
bless and comfort her. Prayed the Lord to
bless all the sisters.
   Singing, Doxology.   Benediction by


[Column 1]
President Middleton. Meeting adjourned
until two o’clock.
	Afternoon session opened by singing
“Our God we raise to Thee.” Prayer by
President Middleton. Singing, “Glorious
things are sung of Zion.”
	President Middleton spoke again to the
sisters for a few minutes. Said he had been
very much interested in what had been
said here today, and he was thankful that
we lived in these days of prophets, for he
had been acquainted with all. He loved
all the societies of the Church and enjoyed
visiting the same.
	Sister E. B. Wells was pleased to speak
in another Stake of Zion on the subject ot
her trip to England. She spoke also of her
coming into the Church and listening to
the Prophet Joseph, said he was the most
wonderful man she ever saw. She testified
of his divine mission and the great work he
established. Felt that the sisters did not
realize the importance of this society. Said
there were now thirty thousand women in
six hundred branches of the Relief Society.
Thought the teachers were very, important
persons, and should always be able to re-
port. Said she felt that Weber county was
a banner Stake, being organized twenty-
two years last July. Asked the sisters to
remember Sister Zina D. H. Young, Sister
Richards and Sister Smith in their prayers.
Thought we should all make an effort to
keep our young people in the faith. Al-
luded to the Temples and the future Tem-
ple that will be built in Jackson County.
Spoke of the good the young women were
doing in the missionary work, speaking out
doors, distributing tracts, etc. She spoke
also of the convention at Washington last
winter, and the question of voting against
the Robert’s Resolution. She then gave a
very pleasant account of her visit to the
Queen; said thirteen out of fifty-three dele-
gates from the United States were from
Utah.
	Counselor Harriet Brown felt that we
had been blest, and she rejoiced in the good
things we had heard but still we mourn
the loss of President Richards.
	Singing, “Lord dismiss us.” Benediction
by President Middleton. Conference ad-
journed for three months.
				C. E. DRIVER, Sec.
SALT LAKE STAKE.
	The quarterly conference of the Relief
Society of the Salt Lake Stake was held in
the Assembly Hall, December 23, 1899.
	Present on the stand were President M. I.
Horne, Counselors Annie T. Hyde and
Clara C. Cannon, Presidents B. W. Smith
and Sarah J. Cannon, of the General Board,
Sister Julia C. Howe, one of the aids, also
Sisters Rachel R. Grant, Maria W. Wilcox
and E. J. Stevenson.
	As this was the anniversary of the birth
of the Prophet Joseph Smith, the exercises
were, in a great measure, commemorative
of that event.
	The roll call showed seventeen city
wards and eleven country wards represen-
ted. The conference coming at the holiday
season, and the weather being unfavorable,
prevented many from attending, and the
conference was not so well attended as
could have been desired.
	Conference opened by singing “We thank
Thee, O God, for a prophet.” Prayer by
Sister Julia C. Howe. Singing, “Our God
we raise to Thee.”
	President Horne spoke of the necessity of
order and punctuality in attending to our

[Column 2]
duties. She was sorry to see no more
present on this occasion, when, in addition
to our regular conference, we had met to do
honor to our Prophet. Spoke of the pro-
posed division of our Stake and said this
would be, perhaps, the last time we would
meet as a whole Stake, and wished to hear
expressions from the sisters in regard to the
matter of division. 
	The followihg sisters spoke on the sub-
ject: Sister Jensen, of Sandy; Sister Mary
A. Dixon, of Mountain Dell; Sister L. S.
Stevenson, of Forest Dale; Sister Sarah J.
Cannon, of Cannon Ward. All expressed
themselves as prepared to be satisfied with
the change, though in some instances it
would cause inconvenience.
	Sisters Bathsheba W. Smith and Rachel
R. Grant spoke of the divine mission of the
Prophet Joseph Smith and bore strong
testimonies of the same. Gave personal
reminiscences of his life, and their knowl-
edge of him.
	President Horne wanted the opinion of
the sisters in regard to holding our meet-
ings every three months. President Young
established this order and she felt like con-
tinuing it, unless the presiding brethren
advised us to hold our conference every six
months.
	Conference adjourned until 2 p. m. Meet-
ing closed by singing the Doxology, and
benediction by Counselor Annie T. Hyde.
	Afternoon session opened with the sing-
ing of the hymn, “Hail to the prophet,”
by members of the Temple choir, under the
leadership of Elder C. J. Thomas.  Open-
ing prayer by Elder Samuel Richards.
Following, Brother Horace S. Ensign sang,
in a very effective manner, the beautiful
hymn, “The Seer.” Reading of the min-
utes of the afternoon session of the confer-
ence held September 2, 1899, accepted.
	President Horne felt that the sisters
should have made arrangements to attend
this commemoration of the birth of the
Prophet. They could make some sacrifices
for this special occasion. She had been
well acquainted with the Prophet, and had
entertained him at her home many times.
Related many incidents of an interesting
character in connection with these visits.
	Brother Samuel Richards spoke at some
length on the life and works of the Prophet
Joseph Smith. Told of some occasions on
which he spoke with such power and force
that every one could feel and know that he
was inspired by God. Brother Richards
spoke in a very interesting manner, and
claimed the closest attention from his
audience.
	Brother George D. Pyper sang, in a very
feeling manner, “The Holy City,” which
was much appreciated.
	Continuing, Brother Richards related
many personal experiences with the
Prophet, and said that he considered his
mission on earth second only to that of the
Savior, and, like the latter, he gave his life
at last for the truth’s sake.
	The Temple choir sang “Weep not, weep
not for me Zion.”
	President Angus M. Cannon offered an
apology for his late entrance. Another
meeting had detained him, and had he not
promised to attend, he would not have been
here, but would have been in bed, as he
was not feeling well. The impression made
by the Prophet on his youthful mind would
never be forgotten. He was truly a prophet
of the Lord, and Brigham Young followed,
fulfilling his part, as did also John Taylor
and Wilford Woodruff. “May the bless-

[Column 3]
ings of the Lord be with us all, with Sister
Horne, your president. This may be the
last time we will meet all together as one
Stake, for by the 1st of February there may
be two or three Stakes formed from what
was originally the Salt Lake Stake. And
thus Zion is growing, and the Lord’s will
be done.”
	Sister Lula Greene Richards felt that she
would be doing an injustice to her feelings
and her conscience if she did not accept the
invitation given her by Sister Horne to
testify to the divine calling of Joseph Smith;
and she knew, as had been stated, that
none stood between him and God, except
the Savior. “I hope I shall never be afraid
to bear this testimony before all the world.”
	Conference adjourned until the first
Saturday in March. After singing, bene-
diction was offered by President Angus M.
Cannon.
		AUGUSTA W. GRANT, Sec. 
HAWAIIAN MISSION.
	The semi-annual conference of the Relief
Society of the Hawaiian mission met in the
Laie meeting house, Saturday morning,
October 7, 1899, President Alice R. Wool-
ley presiding.
	The meeting commenced by singing,
“How firm a foundation.” Prayer by Sister
Luukia Pono. Continued by singing, “O,
my Father.” Minutes of the previous
April conference were read and accepted.
	President Alice R. Woolley, the first
speaker, was pleased to meet again in our
Relief Society conference. She was glad
to labor in the work of God, and knew we
had received the true Gospel and the true
light, whereby we can work out for our-
selves eternal life. Spoke of the necessity
of attending the Relief Society and the
benefits gained thereby. Exhorted the
sisters to take care of the poor and needy
among them, for that is the true work of
the Relief Society. The study of the Gos-
pel should also be carried on in the meet-
ings, and we should live humble and
prayerful lives at all times. Asked God to
bless us all while we were assembled to-
gether, that we might have a time of re-
joicing.
	Sister Kai Kaimiawa reported Laie society
and gave a brief sketch of the work since
its organization.
	Sister Kamopama Wahilani reported
Awaiolimu society to be in a prosperous
condition. Said they study the Gospel,
take care of the poor and assist the needy,
and also take care of the dead. Spoke of
giving alms, and referred to the 6th chapter
of Mathew on the subject.
	Sister Laie Broad reported her mission-
ary labors in Maui in connection with Sister
Jennie Musser. Said the majority of the
branches are in a good, condition, most of
them anxious to carry on the work that is
resting upon them. She was thankful she
had been chosen to labor for God and His
cause.
	Sister Jennie P. Musser felt to sustain
the former speaker in what she had re-
ported. She had enjoyed a good time in
her labors and had wanted for nothing.
Was proud to have the privilege of preach-
ing the Gospel of Jesus Christ, and exhort-
ed all to be faithful and keep the com-
mandments of God.
	Duet, by Misses Hattie Kekuku and
Annie Apua.
	An essay on Relief Society work was.
then read by Sister Alice Smith.


Singing, “Hail to the brightness of
Zion’s glad morning.’’    Prayer by Sister
Kamahuilani.
	Afternoon session.  Meeting opened by
singing  “Praise to the man who communed
with Jehovah.’’    Prayer by Sister Ruth
Kauhini.   Continued by singing, “We
thank Thee, O God, for a prophet.’’
	Sister Poopu reported Kahana society.
Sister Rebecca Oliver gave a favorable
report of Waikiki Relief Society, and was
glad that she was a helper in the great
work.  Prayed that the Lord would assist
us to be faithful.
	Sister Kahopewai, of Maui, reported the
Waihee society.
	Sister Miliama Kekuku treated the sub-
ject of “Care of Children,’’ and said:   “It
is necessary that we teach our sons and
daughters those things that will help them
to live good and industrious lives. Teach
them the Gospel and those things that will
elevate them, and they will in after life
honor and bless us. We will be held re-
sponsible for the conduct of our children,
and if we fail to teach them the principles
of the Gospel, the condemnation will rest
upon our shoulders.”
	Sister Elizabeth C. Williams said she
was pleased to meet with the Saints in this
capacity, and desired to be directed by the
spirit of God that she might say something
that would be beneficial to all concerned.
Spoke of her labors on the Island of Kauai,
was pleased with the treatment they had
received, and knew that God had blessed-
them with His spirit while they were
laboring on that Island.   Was thankful to
have the privilege of preaching the Gospel.
Satan’s time is short, and he is diligent to
gain power over us.   But by diligence and
humility we can gain the assistance of the
spirit of the Lord to keep us in the line of
our duty; for “what value if we gain the
whole world and lose our own souls?”
Essay, “Salvation for the Dead,” by Sis-
tnr Kapili Luahiwa, of Waikiki.
	Sister Victoria Kakelaka spoke of her
labors on Kauai in connection with Sister
Elizabeth C. Williams.
	Sister Annie Kauluken gave a report of
her labors performed in North Hawaii and
Hilo Conference.   Spoke of how they had
gone among those not of our faith and
taught them the principles of the Gospel.
Referred to the blessings attached to the
observance of the Word of Wisdom.
	Sister Minerva Fernandez spoke upon
tithing and the counsel given by the ser-
vants of God on that subject in Zion, and
expressed her desire to fulfill the require-
ments of that law and gain the blessings
promised to all who obey this command-
ment.
	Sister Kealoha Reese, of Waikiki, sus-
tained the report given by Sister Kauluken
in regard to their labors on the Island of
Hawaii.    Said they had enjoyed their
labors and the spirit of the Lord had been
with them.
	Sister Ellen C. Cole said she was pleased
to meet in our conference again under such
favorable circumstances.   “We have heard
many precious things this day concerning
our work here on earth, so let us be faith-
ful and weary not in the good fight we
have begun.”   Spoke of the necessity of
parents teaching their children what is
right.
	Duet, by Sisters Margarett Fifield and
Jennie Musser.
Sister Margarett Fifield said, " I am
pleased to meet together again with you
this afternoon. It is the desire of every
soul to worship some Supreme being, but to
us is revealed the true Being.”    Urged all
to be faithful.
	President Alice R. Woolley said, “I am
pleased with what has been said during the
hours of this day, and my heart is filled
with gratitude to the Lord for His blessings
to us while we have been assembled to-
gether in our General Conference; and I
pray that He will assist us to remember the
things we have heard and aid us to put
them in practice, that we may prepare our-
selves for the great labor that will devolve
upon us while we sojourn here in mortality.
We should be liberal in donating, that we
may have more in the treasury to assist in
carrying on this great work we are engaged
n.
Singing,  “Do what is right.” Benedic-
tion by Elder Thomas Waddups.
                 Alice R. Woolley, Pres.
                 Elizabeth E. Williams, Sec.

WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


[Col
JOSEPA ITEMS. 
	The Josepa Relief Society has just been
bereft of one of its most prominent and in-
fluential members, Sister Makanoe Trous-
seau, who has only been in Utah one year
and a half, but by her energy and good
works she had become well established in
the colony and was ever a power for good.
	In the demise of Sister Makanoe the whole
colony have lost a mother and true friend.
She was ever found at the bedside of the
sick, and her association with Dr. Trous-
seau, of Honolulu, had given her much
useful information, and she was an efficient
and untiring helper in all the interests of
the community.
	The Relief Society desire to express their
appreciation of her good works, and would
exhort all to emulate her worthy example.
OBITUARY AND BIOGRAPHY.
	Sister Makanoe was the third child of Mr. and
Mrs. Joseph Koluhilaau, and was born in the
notable town of Hilo, Hawaii, May 11, 1856. She
attended the Hilo day school from the age of six
years till 1863, then removed to Kona with her
parents and there finished her school education in
1868. She lost her mother, and three years later
married Mr. Kaaipa, by whom she had three chil-
dren, two girls and one boy. Miss Hannah
Kaaipa is the oldest child, and has had the advan-
tage of a good education in the English as well as
the Hawaiian language,
	Makanoe separated from her first husband, and
one year afterwards became the wife of Dr. Trous-
seou, of Honolulu. He was the doctor of the royal
family, and she associated very much with them.
In 1884 she accompanied Queen Kapialani and
the ex-queen, Liliuakalani, on their extended
visits around the group of islands. She was quite
at home in the best society, being a noble, digni-
fied and lofty-minded woman. She was a great
lover of her country and its beautiful scenery.
She visited, the volcano several times with her
husband. She gave her children the advantage of a
good education. She was ever noted among her
associates for her intelligence and wisdom.
	In 1890 Makanoe with her two daughters united
themselves with the Church of Jesus Christ of
Latter-day Saints, and, though she met with some
opposition from her friends, she never from that
time forth flinched or in any way neglected her
Church duties. She was the most influential
member of the Honolulu branch, and exercised a
presidency over the Relief Society of the different
Islands. She was chosen president of the Primary
Association in 1895, and still later she and her
daughter, Hannah, traveled for three months,
visiting the Relief Society and different women’s
organizations in our Church. On their return
they reported their labors at the semi-annual con-
ference at Laie (which she always attended) and
Makanoe and Kalwaianne were called on another
similar mission. Her daughter Hannah’s health
was impaired and she took her to the different
Islands to regain her health, and would fast and

[Column 2]
pray for her herself, as well as having her ad-
ministered to.
	Dr. Trousseau died and left Makanoe quite a
fortune. She helped all of her relatives and
donated liberally to the support of the mission. In
the spring of 1898, she, with her father, children
and other relatives, eleven in number, arrived in
Utah and settled in Josepa. That same year she
and her family entered into the Holy Temple and
did the work for themselves and many relatives;
and while in the city for that purpose she had the
pleasure of spending the day in the hotel with her
former friend and queen, Liliuahalani, and intro-
ducing the presidents of the Church and a few
of the sisters to the queen. Sister E. B. Wells,
Dr. E. R. Shipp, Emily G. Cluff and Miss Birdie
Jean Cluff were the only ladies admitted into the
queen’s apartments, aside from Makanoe and her
two daughters, Makanoe spent the whole time
trying to convince the queen of the great blessing
bestowed upon the Latter-day Saints, and obtained
permission from her to do the Temple work for
her relatives for many generations, in fact the
queen sent Makanoe a large book of genealogies
for that purpose, some of which she has done and
now the work falls on Hannah.
	In 1899 Makanoe accompanied President Joseph
F. Smith and party to the Sandwich Islands, at-
tended to her business and some ohurch duties,
and returned with them, only to redouble her
efforts in building up her chosen home. She has
attended all the General Conferences since she
came to Utah and spent weeks in the Tem-
ple.
	On November 30, 1899, she was taken very ill
with cerebral trouble, accompanied with a high
fever. She asked to be taken to Salt Lake City
that she might visit the Temple. Her relatives
and friends protested, as it was a hard journey in
cold weather, but she was determined to go and
they all finally consented, and her brother and
two daughters brought her in on December 7.
On the 8th she visited the House of the Lord and
received blessings that seemed to fully repay her
for her journey, and she seemed resigned and con-
tented and at 6 o’clock on the morning of the 9th,
she passed peacefully away surrounded by her
daughters, President H. H. Cluff and wife, and
Brother and Sister Worthington. All that kind
friends could do was done for her. President
Joseph F. Smith, ever her true friend, and many
of the Island missionaries mingled their sympathies
with the grief-stricken daughters.
	President Joseph E. Taylor, with his corps of
faithful sisters, arranged her in the pure linen and
laid her in a beautiful white casket, and laden
with love and beautiful flowers, she was carried to
her home, accompanied by President H. H. Cluff
and her daughters. Her brother, who had
brought her in, drove the remains home after
leaving the train.
	An impressive funeral was held at Josepa, Dec-
ember 12, at 2 p. m.; and though mourned and
much loved by all who knew her, we know she has
only gone before to receive the reward of the just.
Ellen Hallemanu, Sec.
Emily G. Cluff, Pres.


OBITUARY.
RESOLUTIONS OF RESPECT.
To the memory of Sister Martha B. Taylor, who
	departed this life December 25, 1899.

	Whereas, in the dispensation of Providence, God
has seen fit to call home one of His daughters,
who labored long among her sisters as a counselor
in the Relief Society of the 21st ward of the Salt
Lake Stake of Zion, and 
	Whereas, she has proved herself an efficient
counselor and an effective member of the organiz-
ation in all its various duties, and was one whose
kindness and generosity of spirit brought sunshine
unto many hearts, helped the needy in time of
want, and was in her own life a fitting embodi-
ment of her constant prayer, that she might be
humble and united in all that is good, therefore, be
it
	RESOLVED, That we, her sisters, united with her
in the Relief Society work, do deeply feel her loss
in connection with her earnest effort and constant
work; nevertheless, in this our loss we feel to bow
to the mandates of an Alwise Creator, who doeth
all things well.
	RESOLVED, That a copy of these resolutions be
entered on the minutes of the 21st ward Relief
Society, and that a copy be sent to her family and
one to the WOMAN’S EXPONENT.
		ELIZA BARTON, President.
		FRANCES S. CASTLETON, Counselor.
		M. A. TUDDENHAM, Secretary.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


U.W. P. C.
	The U. W. P. C. held its regular month- 
ly meeting at the home of Mrs. Emma
Jensen, Saturday, December 30, 1899. 
	The rooms were beautifully decorated for
the occasion. The president in the chair.
Opening prayer by Dr. Romania B. Pratt.
The secretary called the roll, members re-
sponding with original sentiments.
	The resignation of Maria Francis as 2nd
vice-president, and also as a member of the
club, was tendered, and accepted with re-
grets. Mrs. E. B. Wells nominated Emily
S. Richards as 2nd vice-president nomina- 
tion seconded by Dr. Pratt and others, and
carried unanimously.
	A communication was read in regard to
collecting reading matter for the soldiers in
the Philippines, and several ladies volun-
teered to respond to tbe call.
	A communication regarding the Kinder-
garten was read.
	Mrs Schiller reported from the Council
of Women’s Clubs, making definite explana-
tions, and several of the ladies expressed their
views on the subject. Mrs. Helen V.
Kimball was nominated delegate to the
Council.
	A motion was made that Mrs. Wells’
dea in regard to the Kindergarten—that it
was not possible to do what was suggested,
and that the school alluded to was strictly a
district school and should be supported by
those in that locality—was accepted and
carried.
	Mention was made of the handsome pic-
ture presented to the retiring president, Mrs.
Ella W. Hyde, at the October meeting.
	Mrs. Wells moved that the remainder of
the time be spent socially, and the motion
was carried. All were very highly enter-
tained examining the very fine collection of
curios gathered from all parts of the world
by the host and hostess, Mr. and Mrs.
Jensen.
	Sumptuous refreshments were afterwards
served, during which the following musical
program was given: Miss Lizzie Thomas
gave an instrumental selection entitled
“Hearts and Flowers.” “Love I have
Sighed to Rest Me,” was beautifully sung
by Miss Judith Anderson. Mrs. Anderson
and her daughter sang “Whispering Hope,”
and Mrs. Anderson sang “Midst Roses
Sweet.” Miss Minerva and Ella Jensen
and Miss Thomas sang “The Gipsy Coun-
tess,” and the program was concluded with
a song, “Flee as a Bird,” by Judith
Andersen.
			GRACE ANDERSON, Sec.


CHRISTMAS.

	Of all the old festivals that of Christmas
awakens the strongest and most heartfelt
associations. There is a tone of solemn and
sacred feeling that blends with our joys and
lifts the spirit to a state of hallowed and ele-
vated enjoyment. Christmas commemor-
ates the religion of peace and love. It has
been made the season for the gathering to-
gether of families and the drawing together
again of those bands of kindred hearts; of
calling back the children who have wan-
dered from the home, once more to gather
around the paternal hearth, there to grow
young again among the loving mementoes
of childhood.
	There is a charm about Christmas that
fills our hearts with mute but exquisite
delight. Our friendly sympathies are


aroused. We feel more sensibly the charm
of each other’s society, and are brought
more closely together by dependence upon
each other for enjoyment. Heart calleth
unto heart, and we draw our pleasures from
the deep wells of loving kindness which lie
in the quiet depth of our hearts.
	Christmas is a day upon which to rejoice
and be happy; a mirthful day, full of inno-
cent pleasures. It throws open every door
and unlocks every heart, and brings all to-
gether in one warm, generous flow of love
and kindness. The presents, the good
cheer, the tokens of love, the evergreen
distributed about the church or home, the
emblems of peace and gladness, all awaken
fond memories and kindle benevolent sym-
pathies.
	How delightedly we turn from the cares of life and mingle in the festivities of the
day! Every sound is full of melody and I
every countenance bright with smiles,
transmitting to others a supreme and ever
shining benevolence, which constitutes a
merry Christmas and reminds us of “Peace
on earth, good will to men.”
	SUSAN E. THOMPSON.


A National Council of Nurses has been
formed in Denmark, and has become affili-
ated with the International Council of
Nurses. The Danish nurses distinguished
themselves during the Greek war.
	MRS. HILDA MINER celebrated her one
hundredth birthday on December 29, at the
home of her daughter, Mrs. H. D. Miner,
in Fredonia, N. Y. She has seven grand-
children, nineteen great-grandchildren and
three great-great-grandchildren. She pos-
sesses a clear mind, good memory, excellent
health and a remarkably amiable and cheer-
ful disposition. Her friends think the pros-
pect bright for a continuance of her long
and useful life. There are two other per
sons in Chautauqua County who have lived
beyond their hundredth birthdays, Ambrose
R. Hanchett, of Mayville, who was a hun-
dred last November, and Mrs. Doty, of
Frewsburg, who is in her one hundredth
and fourth year.
