[Column 1]
THE SPARROW’S MISSION.

Seated alone at my window
	Gazing down on the street, 
I watched the people passing,
	And heard the dull tramp of their feet.
But my heart was o’erwhelmed with sorrow,
	As I cried in my agony,
“Father amid these many millions,
	Tell me, canst Thou know of me?

“Can every one of thy children,
	Claim a share of Thy love?
Can a prayer of mine ascend
	To Thy home in the realms above?”
And my heart was o erwhelmed with longing,
	So I cried in my agony,
“Father, amid these many millions,
	Tell me, dost Thou care for me?”

Just then a sparrow descended,
	Perched on my window sill,
And chirped as he flew away,
	His mission of love to fulfill.
But the bird had taught me a lesson,
	A message from heaven to me,
The Father who cares for the sparrows,
	Does know and will care for thee. 
				MARY F. KELLY.

RESULTS OF THE REVOLUTION.
	(Read at a meeting of the Daughters of the Re-
volution.)
	The revolution in 1776 was one of the
most important events in human history.
It related not only to the freedom from
tyranny of the people who took the initia-
tive and carried it to a successful issue, but
to the liberties ot the human race as a
whole. The history of the world from then
to the present proves this, if it proves any-
thing.
	This momentous occurrence produced
the most perfect basis upon which to build
a beneficient governmental superstructure—
the American Constitution. It has no
counterpart in human affairs. It provides
to the individual citizen the fullest possible
freedom, the most ample personal protec-
tion and the complete security of legal
property possession. It is the basis guar-
anty of exact equality before the law,
without classified distinctions. Hence the
American nation is composed of the most
independent and, therefore, the most strong-
ly individualized race on the face of the
earth today, with a record for progress that
has no parallel.
	The leaven of the American Revolution
and its results have not been combined
within the borders of this nation, but have
stimulated emulation in every civilized
country, and a degree of its genius has

[Column 2]
even been carried into nations hitherto en-
veloped in the shroud of absolute barbarism.
	But perhaps the most striking beneficient
effects have been produced upon the nation
from which the American colonies revolted
—the kingdom of Great Britain. This
nation is now but a monarchy in name.
The estimable lady who nominally occu-
pies the throne of the British empire is
practically subject to the will of the minis-
terial government, her prerogatives of
sovereignty being mostly mere matters of
form.
	Similar changes in a more modified de-
gree have been developed in all of the
great nations, even among those which
were heretofore unqualifiedly despotic.
The central course of this remarkable ten-
dency toward popularization of rule is com-
prised in the American Revolution; and its
results—the production of the constitution,
the development of institutions of govern-
ment in harmony with that magnificent
basis, and the progressive effects upon the
nation.
	The history of American institutions
shows that every impediment to the devel-
opment of human liberty, as embodied in
our national system, must, in the process of
Divine adjustment of human affairs, sooner
or later be swept out of existence—hence
the civil war of 1861. Its first result was
the preservation of the Union; its second,
the abolition of slavery. The removal of 
that blot upon our escutcheon made the
great Republic consistent within itself, and
placed it in a position to champion, when
necessary, the cause of the oppressed out-
side of its own border, as well as within the
circle of its own domain.
	The nation may pass through many nar-
row places in the future. Trial is a de-
veloper of national as well as Individual
character, but every barrier to her progress
will be surmounted.
	Her past shows that her destiny is to
continue to be the central light of good
government to all the world.
			MABELLE SNOW.


[Column 1]
THOMAS JEFFERSON.
(Read at a meeting of the D. A. R.)
	For the fulfillment of great purposes 
great men are born; and who questions the
idea that the immortal Washington and his 
brilliant and courageous colleagues were pre-
destined for their wonderful careers. Where 
in history can a similar fate to that of 
Washington be found? How he was pro-
tected through the long, fierce struggle of 
the Revolution, both from the enemy’s
fire and the dangers of the camp, though 
he shared all the privations and terrors
with his soldiers and stood always in the 
front of battle, often between the lines 
with fierce and dreadful firing on both sides
yet escaped unharmed, unscathed. His
coat of mail was neither steel, such as was 
worn by the warrior knights of old, nor 
stitched with threads of sorceries and super-
stition, like the knights of mythical legends, 
but an armor of a faith sublime; and a 
knowledge that his cause was just, made 
him invulnerable.

[Column 2]
	Washington stands in the vast firmament 
of American history the brightest star, but 
around his name cluster such a brilliant 
constellation of names as to illumine 
the path of our history for ages yet to 
come, and not the least brilliant of these 
is the subject of this sketch—Thomas Jeffer-
son.
	The only difficulty arising when writing 
a simple sketch of Jefferson is to know 
what not to tell, as for more than forty 
years, from youth to extreme old age, years 
when America was making most wonderful 
history, he was one of the most active and 
notable of Americans. 
	A certain fascination and romance is al-
ways associated with the military, and 
doubtless the most daring and brilliant 
heroes of the Revolution were the men who 
surrounded Washington on the battlefield, 
but in the making of a country the scholar 
and statesman is quite as necessary as the 
soldier, if not more so. It is the mind that 
guides the army, after all, and to quote an 
old familiar phrase, “The pen is mightier 
than the sword.”
	The one statesman above all others to 
whom this country is forever and ever in-
debted is Thomas Jefferson. Jefferson’s
father, Peter Jefferson, with a few other 
pioneers, doubtless anticipating the advice 
of Horace Greely, “to go west and grow up 
with the country,” had left the settlements 
along the James river and taken up home-
steads in a then unoccupied and almost un-
known land, along the Rivama river, just 
east of the Blue Ridge mountains, and here 
on this estate, named Shadwell, Thomas 
Jefferson was born, and here he lived as a 
child, boy and man, with but temporary 
interruptions, for 27 years. For a boy 
born in a wilderness he enjoyed remarkable 
advantages. His biographers tell us he had 
good English at five, Latin, Greek and 
French at nine, regular classical studies at 
fourteen, a college course at seventeen and at 
twenty-four he was admitted to the bar.
	His personal appearance at this time is 
described as six feet two inches in height, 
slim, erect as an arrow, with regular 
features, a very ruddy complexion, an ex-
tremely delicate skin, full, deep-set hazel
eyes and sandy hair. He was more a stud-
ent than an athlete, yet he possessed a pas-
sionate love of nature and took the greatest 
delight in horsemanship. Though an ardent
student, he was not necessarily a book-
worm, but, on the contrary, was fond of 
society. He was an expert musician, the
violin being his favorite instrument, was a 
good dancer and a daring rider.
	His devotion to home life and the build-
ing of his beautiful and famous home of 
Monticello reads like a romance. He had 
his first love affair during his college years.
It was, however, unsuccessful, but it stirred
“his sympathetic nature to its profoundest
depths.” However, he recovered from this
disappointment and later very happily mar-
ried a beautiful, accomplished and wealthy
widow, Mrs. Martha Skelton, whose wealth 
greatly aided him in carrying out his plans 
for the home and estate of Monticello, but 
she did not live to see them all fulfilled. 
	A few words about this beautiful, historic


[Column 1]
home would not be out of place here, for it 
occupied almost all the spare moments of 
thought from public life of its owner. Even 
to this day it is considered a work of art 
and wonderful ingenuity. It stands on a 
hill known as Little Mountain, overlooking 
the whole estate. Mr. Jefferson began the 
building of this home before the Revolu-
tion, but so many interruptions occurred 
that it scarce deserved the name of a home 
until after twelve years labor, in 1782 the 
Marquis de Chalellux paid Jefferson a visit 
and thus describes the house: “The house
Monticello,—of which Mr. Jefferson was 
the architect, and often one of the work-
men—is rather elegant and in the Italian 
taste, though not without fault.” Then
after describing the different rooms he 
further says: “We may safely aver that 
Mr. Jefferson is the first American who has 
consulted the fine arts to know how to shel-
ter himself from the weather.” But in 
course of time the house changed entirely 
and became from a plain Italian villa a
spreading country mansion of twenty 
rooms, with Greek porticoes and an octa-
gonal dome with very solid brick walls, 
strong frame work, good floors, a great pro-
fusion of elaborate trimming and full of his 
quaint and strongly original devices.
	The designing of this house was a labor
of love, and here, when free from public
duties, Jefferson lived, the cherished mem-
ber of a wide circle of friends, the head and 
center of a numerous family group, and 
here were entertained, with the grace and 
hospitality of a true Virginia gentleman,
the most noted people of our own land, and 
the most distinguished writers from foreign
lands. The conduct of this home life clear-
ly illustrates the fact that the artistic and 
poetic temperament dominated, not only
his youth, but his whole life.
	As Thomas Jefferson’s home-life was 
ideal and a beautiful example to young 
America, far more so was his public career, 
For over forty years he served his country 
as legislator, governor, congressman, minis-
ter plenepotentiary, cabinet officer, vice-
president and finally president.
	The most noted work of his life, and the 
one that has immortalized his name and 
cherished it in the hearts of the American
people, is, of course, the Declaration of In
dependence. It is a simple matter of his-
tory, probably, which every school child 
knows, and yet, surely sufficiently impor-
tant and interesting to bear repeating how 
the Declaration was prepared: Jefferson
was named a delegate to the General Con-
gress where he took his seat eight days after 
Colonel Washington had been appointed by 
Congress commander-in-chief of the colo-
nies, and he was placed upon the committee 
to draw up a statement of the causes which 
had impelled the colonies to take up arms 
against the mother country, upon another
committee to report upon Lord North’s con-
ciliatory proposition, when in the winter of 
1775-6 the news arrived from England that 
the king had denounced the colonists as 
rebels, and the law confiscating American 
vessels and cargoes, and the impressment 
of American crews into the British navy 
was authorized, the colonies became more 
united, and on the 11th of June, 1776, Con-
gress appointed Jefferson, Adams, Franklin, 
Sherman and Livingstone to prepare a
Declaration of Independence. Jefferson, at
the request of his associates, prepared a
draft of the Declaration, which, after two
or three verbal corrections by them, was

[Column 2]
taken up for consideration in the House on 
the 2nd of July.
	In the debates on the Declaration Jeffer-
son took no part, “thinking it a duty to 
be, on that occasion, a passive auditor of the 
opinions of others, more impartial judges
than he could be of its merits or demerits.”
Two or three expressions had been made 
which gave offense to some members, the 
words of “Scotch and other foreign auxili-
aries” were resented by some delegates of 
Scottish birth; and the strictures on the
king’s repeated veto of colonial law which 
permitted the slave trade, were disapproved 
by some of the southern delegates. 
	On the evening of the Fourth of July, 
1776, the Declaration was reported back 
from the committee of the whole House 
and agreed to. Thus was framed and 
passed the document which circumstances
have given an importance possessed by no 
other production of an American pen. It is 
regarded as the weapon that dismembered a 
great empire and that gave birth to the 
grandest of nations. For more than a cen-
tury on each anniversary of its adoption it 
has been read to assembled multitudes, and 
its style and sentiment are a model for 
every people which seeks for itself the 
right of self-government. Jefferson him-
self requested “that his tombstone should 
testify it to be the greatest achievement of 
his life.’’ Had others of these early leaders
possessed the sagacity and forethought of 
Jefferson it is more than probable that the 
sorrow and despair of the great civil war
would have been averted, for in the original 
draft of the Declaration there was a clause 
abolishing slavery. Then in the Virginia
legislature he introduced a law which was 
finally passed, prohibiting the further im-
portation of slaves into the state, and in 
Congress, 1783, he introduced a clause pro-
viding that after the year 1800 of the Chris-
tian era, there should be neither slavery 
nor involuntary servitude in any of the 
states, otherwise than the punishment of 
crimes whereof the party shall be duly con-
victed to have been personally guilty.”
And it is a fact that though he was fired 
with the Virginia passion for acquiring 
land he did not try to increase his slaves,
and never owned sufficient number to 
properly care for and till his large posses-
sions. Neither by purchase or investment
did he ever acquire slaves, possessing only 
those inherited from his father’s and wife’s
estates. On this question his own words 
are the best illustration of the depth of his 
feelings; he said: “I tremble for my country
when I think that God is just; that His 
justice cannot sleep forever; that considering 
numbers, nature and natural means only, a 
revolution of the wheel of fortune, an ex-
change of situations is among possible 
events, that it may become probable by 
supernatural interference. The Almighty
has no attribute that can take sides with us 
in such a contest.”
	As a statesman Jefferson was unequaled; 
as a politician he held extreme views and 
differed in many important questions with 
the leaders of his time. He was the most 
conspicuous representative of the Demo-
cratic party while Alexander Hamilton was 
the leader of the Federal, the two great 
schools of American politics. This differ-
ence of opinion certainly never carried any 
particular dissension, for both were mem-
bers of Washington’s cabinet, Jefferson 
secretary of state and Hamilton secretary 
of treasury. Washington very prudently 
sided with neither party, though it is

[Column 3]
popularly believed that his sympathies 
were more with the Federal than the 
Republican leader. He must have held his 
powerful opponent in high esteem for in 
the entrance hall of his beloved Monticello,
opposite his own statue, stood a bust of 
Alexander Hamilton. 
	As Congressman one of his most im-
portant acts was securing the adoption of 
the system of coinage still in vogue. As 
president, a position he held  for two terms, 
doubtless the one act most beneficial to the 
country was the purchase of the territory 
of Louisiana from France. He also suc-
ceeded in greatly reducing the public debt, 
fortifying the seaports, reorganizing and re-
arming the militia and extinguishing 
the Indian’s titles by fair purchase and pro-
moting their emigration beyond the Miss-
issippi.
	Though Mr. Jefferson was pressed to ac-
cept a nomination for a third term, even 
formally requested by the legislatures of 
five states, he promptly refused and re-
tired from public life in 1809, to his home 
of Monticello. But though not politically 
or publically engaged in service, he never-
theless always maintained an interest in all 
affairs tending to the welfare of his coun-
try, and one of his last acts was one of the 
most important—namely, the founding of 
the University of Virginia, of which he has 
been said to say, “I would rather be presi-
dent than president of the states.”
	The simplicity of his character is evi-
denced by the manner in which he rode to 
the hall of Congress on the day of his in-
auguration. Instead of a coach and six, as 
had been the custom, he rode horseback,
without a guard or even a servant in his 
train, hitched the horse’s bridal to a fence 
and walked in without ceremony to receive 
the tenures of the highest office in the land. 
The graciousness of his character can pos-
sibly be best illustrated by the remark he 
made when he took Franklin’s place as 
minister abroad: “I go to succeed Frank-
lin; no one could replace him.” The
tenderness by the simple act of keeping a 
vow made in boyhood to his friend, after-
wards brother-in-law, Abner Carr, by bury-
ing him under his favorite oak on the little 
mountain. This incident is said to have 
originated the little cemetery on the slope 
of Monticello, where the dust of Thomas 
Jefferson now lies in its last repose.
			ANNIE W. CANNON.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


MRS. LOUISE MAUDE is the only author-
ized translator of Tolstoi’s great novel
“Resurrection.” A remarkable fact con-
cerning this transaction is that it is the
only complete reproduction of the author’s 
manuscript. The Russian edition has been 
emasculated by the censors so that it can-
not be read in full in the original language.
	SENATOR DEPEW, in his address to the
Sons of the Revolution on Washington’s
Birthday, said that Washington was a
brilliant success in everything except
courtship. He defeated the British many
times, at many places; he stormed forts 
and captured them; but when it came to 
making a maiden’s heart capitulate, he 
failed. On three different occasions, he
said, Washington attempted this, but met 
defeat; and it was not till he met the
Widow Custis that he succeeded. Wash-
ington’s ill success in this direction the
speaker attributed to the fact that he re-
sorted to poetry. When the girls read his
halting verses they concluded that they 
did not want such a suitor.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


A HYMN.

		O, Saints of the Most High,
			Who dwell on Europe’s shore,
		You’ve heard His servant’s cry,
			Low bow His thrown before. 
Praise give to Jesus King, He comes on earth to reign,
Soon will the tidings ring, give glory to His, name.

		O, keep your covenants true,
			You made beneath the wave,
		A crown He’ll give to you,
			And life beyond the grave.
A name and place in Zion, awaits you over there,
And Judah’s fierce lion, will soon your way prepare

		Proud Babylon will fall,
			Destruction o’er her spread;
		List! to the watchmen’s call,
			Have Saints to Zion fled?
The sun will darkened be the moon be turned to
	blood,
The Saints will Jesus see beyond Atlantic’s flood.

		Rejoice ye Saints of God,
			For mighty is His power,
		Hold fast the Iron Rod,
			’Tis His avenging hour.
No more rejected there past the cold finger’s scorn,
O’er the power of men, hail Church of the First
	Born.
						L. D. ALDER.
	London, Eng., Oct. 1899.


[Column 1]
THE RISE AND PROGRESS OF THE
RELIEF SOCIETY.
	As I am not able by sickness to attend
the Relief Society meetings with my sisters,
thought I would employ my pen to write
a short essay.
	Joseph Smith, the prophet of the last
days, was the first to organize a Relief
Society in this last dispensation of the ful-
ness of times. I will here quote a few
lines from his teachings to the Relief
Society.
	He said: “This is a charitable society
and according to your natures, it is natural
for females to have feelings of charity and
benevolence. You are now placed in a
situation in which you can act according to
those sympathies which God has placed in
your bosoms. If you live up to those
principles how great and glorious will be
your reward in the celestial kingdom. If
you live to your privileges, the angels
cannot be restrained from being your
associates. Females, if they are pure and
innocent, can come into the presence of
God: for what is more pleasing to God
than innocence? And I now turn the key
in your behalf in the name of the Lord,
and this Society shall rejoice and knowl-
edge and intelligence shall flow down from
this time henceforth. This is the begin-
ning of better days to the poor and needy,
who shall be made to rejoice and pour forth
blessings on your heads.”
	Find this in the EXPONENT, September
1, 1884.
	Now there are many references made to
the relief of the poor in the Bible. There
is a beautiful illustration of the woman
who used the precious ointment to anoint
the Savior’s body. He said “for His
burial.” His disciples found fault with
the woman, said it was waste, it might
have been sold for so much and given to
the poor. It might have been intended
for that purpose, but she had kept it and
was inspired to pour it upon the head of
Jesus, and He said “she had wrought a
good work on Him,” and wheresoever this
Gospel should be preached in the whole
world, that should be told as a memorial of
her. He also said “For ye have the poor
always with you; but me ye have not al-
ways.”
	Dorcas was one of those women full of

[Column 2]
good works and alms deeds that Peter
brought to life. All the widows stood by
him weeping and showing the coats and
garments which Dorcas made while she
was with them. The things that Dorcas
made were kept to clothe the poor, like our
faithful sisters do and have done. I know
a great many who have spent a lifetime
laboring for the poor in our Relief Society,
faithful from the first organization until
the present.
	There has been a marked degree of im-
provement and progression in our society
from the days of Nauvoo when we heard
the Prophet’s prophetic words. You, my
sisters, the pioneers of the Relief Society,
are the ones that the blessings fall upon;
for at that time all were poor and needy,
and you are they who have been faithful to
your calling and have poured forth bless-
ings upon the heads of the poor, and by so
doing have received their’s in return.
	Who were the beginners? I can count
out of the families of the first members of
the Church, and some few are acting now.
They should berelieved by younger sisters.
And I repeat Joseph Smith’s words:
“Great will be your reward in the celestial
kingdom, and those who have passed be-
hind the veil—yes, the angels—will be
your assistants to help you to follow up 
your lineage to weld the link of parantage
and blood kinship to prepare them for the
millennium reign. Your work has ex-
tended its influence and usefulness to every
Stake and branch of the Church, which be-
speaks the approval of divine authority.
			MARY ANN M. PRATT.
	March 16, 1900.


THE NEW STAKE.
	President Frank Y. Taylor is to make a
visiting tour accompanied by all the officers
of the Granite Stake of Zion, visiting
every ward in the new Stake, leaving home
every Sunday morning in order to attend
the Sunday Schools, afternoon services and
call a conjoint meeting of the Y. M. and Y.
L. M. I. A. Association, for the purpose of
becoming better acquainted with the people
of the different wards.
	After the afternoon services a special call
is to be made for the officers and mem-
bers of the Relief Society to meet and be-
come acquainted with the new Stake presi-
dent of the Relief Society and her officers.
	The first visit was made to the Sugar
Ward, February 11, 1900, Sunday morn-
ing at 10 a. m. After the opening exer-
cises the Stake officers of the Sunday
School and some of the General Board were
introduced. Remarks, instructive and in-
teresting were made to officers, teachers and
children. The children marched to their
different classes in excellent order, accom-
panied with very pleasing music. The
Stake officers were arranged in groups and
conducted to the different classes. We
were very much impressed with the exer-
cises of the kindergarten class, which was
managed with skill and interest.
	After the Sunday School was dismissed
we were ushered into another room of the
building and seated at tables beautifully
decorated with flowers and heavily spread
with a sumptuous dinner, which added to
the comfort and pleasure of all present.
The dinner was conducted by the ladies
in a very pleasing manner. Appropriate
toasts were given by President Taylor and
others.
	The afternoon services were indeed a


spiritual feast. Introductory speeches
were made by both brothers and sisters of
the Stake officers, interspersed with sweet
music by the choir.
	At the close of the meeting President
Taylor requested all the members of the
Relief Society to remain for a short meet-
ing, and introduced Sister Emma Wood-
ruff as president of the Granite Stake Relief
Society She made a few remarks, then
introduced her counselors and secretary,
each of whom expressed a desire to be a
stay and a help to their president, Sister
Woodruff.
	The Relief Society president of Sugar
Ward, Sister Sarah B. Gibson, expressed
her love for Sister Woodruff, and spoke of
her steadfastness in the Gospel from her
(Sister Gibson’s) earliest recollection.
	President Taylor spoke very encourag-
ingly to the sisters. He expressed a deep
interest for the prosperity of the work in
this the new Stake.
	Counselor Miller endorsed all the presi-
dent had said, and made a few closing re-
marks.
	Benediction by Counselor Julia A.
Rockwood, of Sugar Ward
	We very much appreciated the kind and
hospitable treatment tendered us by the
Bishopric and people of the Sugar Ward.
			C. A. S. YOUNG, Sec.


ANNIVERSARY MEETING AT PROVO.
	The members of the Relief Society of the
four wards of Provo City appropriately
celebrated the fifty eighth anniversary of
the organization of the Relief Society, in
Nauvoo, 1842.
	A meeting was held in the Stake Taber-
nacle on Saturday, March 17, at 2 p, m., at
which Sister Mary John presided. After
singing and prayer, a very interesting pro
gram was carried out as follows:
	An address on the organization of the
Relief Society, by Sister Marilla Daniels.
	Items of interest in her life were given by
Sister Martha Bullock.
	Brother James E Daniels sang “Daugh-
ters of Zion”
	Sentiment, “The Relief Society,” by
Sister M. A. Till
	Address by Sister Bennett, subject “The
Organization of Women.”
	Song, by Miss Julia Ramsey.
	Reminiscences of the life of the Prophet
Joseph Smith, taken from the diary of the
late Dr John Riggs, by Sister Melissa
Riggs Stewart.
	An address by Sister Deborah Billings.
	Song by Brother George Buries, “Let
Bygones be Bygones Forever.”
	An address by Sister Sarah Partridge.
	There was a good attendance at the
meeting, and there were ten sisters present
who had seen the Prophet Joseph Smith.
	After singing the Doxology, and prayer
by Brother A. J. Stewart, the meeting was
adjourned for one year.
			CAROLINE B. PRATT.


A FEW months ago it was announced
that both Houses of Parliament in West
Australia had passed a resolution in favor
of full suffrage for women. This was an
earnest that they would pass a bill to that
effect as soon as the necessary red tape
could be untied. Now the news comes
that the bill has finally passed both
houses. So, one by one, the Australian
provinces join the procession.

RELIEF SOCIETY CONFERENCE.
	The General Conference of the Relief
Society will be held in the Salt Lake
Assembly Hall in this city, on Thursday,
April 5, meetings commencing at 10 a. m.
and 2 p. m. It is desired that there should
be a large attendance of the members, and
that each Stake organization should be
represented by its president, or some one
authorized to act for her. A cordial in-
vitation is extended to the Bishops and all

[Column 1]
OUR NEW POSSESSIONS
	It is hard work to settle the question of
the right kind of government for the new
possessions, as they are called, that have
recently been made over to the United
States, even in one’s own mind. Various
propositions are made as to what ought to
be done, and women have had something to
say, although women’s opinion on national
questions is not what is expected by men of
this nation. Nevertheless, women have
opinions as well as intuitions and emotions.
	It is rather confusing to one who would
gladly take a correct view of the situation
to hear so many sides to this important
question argued. The Hon. Whitelaw
Reid, in what is said by good judges to
have been an able and impressive address,
stated at the close of his speech, “My last
word is an appeal to stand firm and stand
altogether for the continental union and for
a firm civil service for the islands.” Presi-
dent Schurman, the great speaker at the
same meeting in Boston, closed his address
by saying, “We must have a permanent
civil service in the Phillipines. It must be
well paid, and the best men in the country
must go there, and they must be animated,
not by the love of money, although they
must have good salaries, but by devotion to
the public service, by considerations for the
welfare of the Filipinos themselves. With
such a class of men going there, retained 
permanently, and promoted in virtue of
merit, I have no doubt whatsoever of the

[Column 2]
successful solution of the problem which
has devolved upon us in the Orient.”
	One editor who has studied this question
carefully, and who lives in a city famous
for its public advantages, comments thus:
“Unfortunately, such a civil service we
have not, never have had and are not likely
to have. He must be indeed an optimist,
devoid of practical observation and exper-
ience, who will hope for good government
by officials appointed by any political party
in power at Washington. The result will
be a carpet-bag government followed by a
social and political collapse. We can
adopt the second alternative and make
these people citizens, not of the United
States, but of Hawaii, Porto Rico of Luzon
and the other islands organized as self-
governing territories.”
	In short, this writer would give these
people a similar form of government to New
Mexico, and of that which formerly was
provided for Utah. Governor and judges
appointed by the President of the United
States; he reasons that this system with free
commercial intercourse with the United
States inside the American custom house,
the free interchange of temperate and torrid
products would bind the new possessions to
us and to each other in a friendly and
mutually profitable unity, material and
moral. This may be good as far as it goes,
but these people must have something else
besides commerce they must have material
and practical civilizing influences and meth-
ods they must have teaching and training
along many lines to make them truly citi-
zens at all. In short, women as well as
men must help with this great undertaking.
The women of those countries will help
wonderfully by their influence when they
have been reached by the women of the
United States, who can be sent out by the
President and Congress to aid in these at-
tempts to rescue from barbarism these
savage nations and tribes.
	The National Council of Women of the
United States appealed to President Mc-
Kinley in February, 1899, that there be a
commission of women to co-operate with
the men who might probably be sent to
these islands when the war was over.
	Women should study these great and
grave questions, even though they do not
form an opinion, it is well to know what
others think and become acquainted with
what the leaders of this great free govern-
ment are doing to help on the cause of en-
lightened freedom.

	At present the war between Great
Britain and South Africa is engaging the
attention more than any other subject. We
are too far removed from the scene of action
to be sure of our opinion, but we must
necessarily deplore such a war, seemingly a
war for greed; and, O, how terrible are the
tragedies being enacted! and who shall
answer for the loss of so many innocent
lives? General Joubert is reported dead,
Gen. Conje has been taken prisoner, which
seems even worse than dying for the free-
dom of one’s country. A brave soldier, a
hero, whatever else may be said of him, it
must indeed be hard for such a man to be
shut up in prison. But with these two
great Generals absent, one in death and one
in prison, it naturally seems the Boers
must soon surrender altogether, and yet,
perhaps, there are others as brave and as
skillful, and the war may go on, for if, as
Olive Schriener says, the people of the
Transvaal are determined to fight until

[Column 3]
death, then there will be more battles
fought, and the end is not yet for the great
armies of Great Britain. 
	Would that some peaceable method of
settling hostilities might be negotiated and
the peace which Shakespeare speaks of in
Henry IV, might follow:
	“Peace is in the nature of a conquest:
	For then both parties nobly are subdued,
	And neither party losses.”

And Milton says in one of his sonnets:
	“Peace hath her victories,
	No less renowned than war.”

	The latest dispatches show that the Boers
are not conquered, and although the
British soldiers outnumber them by many
thousands undoubtedly, and are well
equipped, yet Kruger has not lost heart, if
reports can be relied upon. He is reported
as saying to the New York World re-
porter: “My burghers are fighting bravely
and they will continue to do so until Great
Britain asks for peace or withdraws her
soldiers,” and adds:
	“Even as your forefathers fought against
great odds in the Revolutionary war, so
we are struggling, and even as God was
with your people, so is he with us. We
have fought with England before and we
defeated her soldiers, and we will be vic-
torious again.”
	But that will be as the Lord wills, He
holds the destinies of nations in His hands,
and His purposes must be fulfilled. But
for women whose dearest and best must go
forth to battle, surely they will with one
accord long and pray for peace and protest
against the horrors of war that fill the earth
with mourning aud lamentation.


EDITORIAL NOTES.
	We have given considerable space to the
George Washington Association because of
the call made and the money raised by the
school children of Utah for the Memorial
Building to be erected in Washington, D.
C. It is well to let the people here and the
children know how far the matter has ad-
vanced and what is really being accom-
plished.
	Tuesday, March 20, was open day at the
Cleofan and the visitors were entertained
delightfully at the home of Mrs. James H.
Moyle. Mrs. Cora Hooper Eldridge gave
a lecture on Oriental Art, illustrated with
rugs, pottery, pictures and dainty articles
that made the subject exceedingly inter-
esting, as was also the conversation at the
time tea was served after the lecture was
over. The Cleofan seems to be quite a pro-
gressive club of exceptionally bright young
women, and many of its members are giving
marked attention to the fine arts.
	The Countess of Derby laid the founda-
tion-stone of the Rectory at St. Albans,
Cheatwood, England. The Earl, her hus-
band, was present. The St. Albans Mis-
sion Guild cleared the ground, the ladies
provided tea for the diggers each Saturday
afternoon until the work was done. This
ceremony performed by a woman is new in
the world; we have had one instance only
of which the writer is aware here in Utah.
President Sarah M. Kimball laid the corner
stone of the Fifteenth Ward Relief Society
hall in the year 1868.
	Sisters M. G. Clawson and P. J. Kimball
gave a Kensington on Tuesday, March 20,
at Sister Clawson’s residence. About sixty


[Column 1]
ladies were present, all old friends, making
the affair a very entertaining one indeed.
Carnations and smilax were used in the
decorations, which made the beautiful par-
lors even more attractive. Sister Clawson
has for years been famous for her dainty
culinary accomplishments, and, of course,
the “tea” was exceptionally fine, and the
ladies lingered around the artistically ar-
ranged tea tables, chatting of other days,
longer than etiquette requires. Both Mrs.
Clawson and her Sister, Mrs. Kimball, are
charming hostesses.
	The Seventeenth of March was pretty
generally observed here and it is ardently
hoped that it was also in other Stakes of
Zion. Condensed history of the Society
itself and sketches of its founders, and
items from the teachings of Joseph Smith
the Prophet, were among the exercises on
the program in some of the wards, together
with music, hymns and songs suitable to
the occasion. In some places parties were
held and dancing was indulged in, and in
others social reunions were given and the
old folks were feasted and made happy.
The day has been observed according to
the wish of the General Board and it has
doubtless made an impression that will be
lasting in the minds of the people. The
17th being Saturday, it was more conven-
ent for some of the branches to celebrate
on Friday, and as each organization could
choose what hour seemed most suitable,
therefore invited guests had the opportunity
to visit several of the meetings.
	The Fifteenth Ward Relief Society held
its meeting in the new hall which the sis-
ters have had finished and furnished during
the past year. It is quite commodious and
was neatly and appropriately decorated.
Mrs. H. A. Badger presided and about
seventy-five members, including a few
visitors, were present. Remarks were
made by President M. Isabella Horne and
Elder L. John Nuttall, and among the
exercises Mrs. E. B. Wells gave a verbal
sketch of the late president of the Society,
Sister S. M. Kimball and read a poem by
Sister L. L. Greene Richards, written for
the Society, and also an address by Presi-
dent Eliza R. Snow, made in July, 1868.
At intervals music and recitations were
rendered. Sister Prudie Brown read a
poem written especially for the meeting by
Sister Mary E. Morris, who was unavoid-
ably kept at home by illness. The musical
exercises, conducted by the choir leader,
Brother Ashman, were exceptionally good.
	The Third Ward Relief Society held its
anniversary meeting to celebrate the first
organization in Nauvoo, on Saturday,
March 17, at 2 p. m., in the meeting
house, Mrs. Frances Smith presiding. The 
secretary had charge of the program.
Visitors present were President M. Isabella
Horne and her counselors, Annie T. Hyde
and Clara C. Cannon, also Elizabeth J.
Stevenson and E. B. Wells, of the General
Board. During the rendering of the music-
al exercises each of the visitors addressed
the meeting. President Horne spoke
mostly upon the signs of the times, quoting
many passages of scripture and prophecies
that are now being fulfilled. The other
subjects referred to were general, though
replete with testimony of the Gospel and
the duties and high calling of the women
of Zion. Sister Wells also alluded to the
advice given by President Z. D. H. Young
in regard to reading the Book of Doctrine

[Column 2]
and Covenants in the Society meetings and
especially the subjects upon faith. Bishop
Maycock made brief remarks at the close
of the meeting. Tables were set in the
vestry where all present partook of deli-
cious refreshments prepared by the Society.
Beautiful flowers adorned the hall and the
good spirit was manifest in all the teach-
ings. President Frances Smith and her
associates are to be congratulated on the
success of their celebration.
	The Twelfth Ward Relief Society con-
vened on Friday evening, in their ward
chapel, President Julia Druce presiding,
and conducting the exercises. The first
feature of the entertainment was dinner
served by a committee of the sisters, who
proved themselves very efficient in the
matter of pleasing their guests. At the
head of one of the long tables sat Bishop
H. B. Clawson and at his left the venerable
president of the Society at large, Sister
Zina D. H. Young, and next to her Sister
Emily Young Clawson, and on the Bishop’s
right was Apostle John Henry Smith and
wife. Sisters E. B. Wells, Julia Druce and
her counselors, J. R. Midgley, Sister
Hooper and others. The feast was abund-
ant and delicious and the conversation
general and interesting. The program was
varied and some of the exercises were
humorous in the extreme, songs and music
very good, and the entertainment passed off
well. Aunt Zina, as she is familiarly
known and lovingly spoken of among the
Saints, made an exceedingly interesting
address, also Apostle John Henry Smith,
Elder L. J. Nuttall and Bishop Clawson
spoke briefly. Sister Midgley read a paper
on the history of the Society and gave a
brief outline of the work of the Society in
that ward, and the several presidents and
officers that had served at different periods
since its organization. The music was
particularly good. We are sorry not to
have space to give the program more fully,
but we can say they have excellent talent
in the Twelfth Ward.
	The Seventeenth Ward Relief Society
celebrated anniversary day March 17,
Saturday evening in the ward hall, Presi-
dent Clarissa S. Williams presiding. The
house was filled with the brethren and sis-
ters and a few invited guests. The sing-
ing was a beautiful feature of the program,
some exquisite songs were rendered, beside
the sacred hymns. Sister B. W. Smith,
one of the presidents of the General Board,
gave a verbal sketch of the first Relief
Society organized in Nauvoo and of its
officers and work. At the close of Sister
Smith’s address, a neat little girl in white
came forward and presented her with an
elegant bouquet of choice flowers. Sister
Julia C. Howe, who had been connected
with the ward since its organization, read a
sketch of the Relief Society in that ward
from the time it was, first organized, men-
tioning in an affectionate manner the sever-
al officers now departed. The secretary,
Mrs. C. F. Wilcox read a written paper on
The life and labors of “Aunt Zina,”
and the possibilities of the Society, and
paid a beautiful tribute to Aunt Zina, her
labors of love, her tender, sympathetic
nature, her untiring devotion to the inter-
ests of Zion, and her magniminity of char-
acter in all departments of life. The presi-
dent responded in a gracious and humble
manner. Sister E. B. Wells, Apostle John
Henry Smith, Elder L. John Nuttall and
Bishop W. J. Beatie each made brief re-

[Column 3]
marks. Mrs. Williams is an excellent pre-
siding officer and is well beloved in her
ward. Towards the close of the evening’s
exercises a very pretty small silk flag was
presented to Sisters Wells and Williams by
the little girl in white.
	We were very much gratified with the
invitation to attend the sisters’ conference
of Juab Stake, at Nephi, and participate in
the reorganization of the Stake Board of
the Relief Society. President Mary Pitch-
forth, who has had charge of affairs
for seventeen years, had resigned, and that
necessitated a new Board of officers. Apostle
George Teasdale was present at the confer-
ence and spoke in high terms of praise and
commendation of President Pitchforth and
of President Goldsbrough, who preceded
her in the position, and the good sisters
who had labored with them both. Sister
S. A. Cazier sang, very sweetly, “Ever re-
membered for what she has done.” Presi-
dent J. W. Paxman, Counselor C. H.
Sperry and Bishop Parks were all present
at the meeting. The new Board consists of
president, Kate L. Paxman, counselors,
S. A. Cazier and Mary A. Grover; aids, M.
A. May, and S. K. Latimer; secretary,
Una Chappell; assistant secretary, Sarah
Brown; treasurer, Helen Grace. Sister
Mary Pitchforth, the retiring president,
and her counselor, Hannah Jones, were
made Honorary members of the Stake
Board, and a hearty vote of thanks was
given them for their faithful Services in the
past. Conference was held on Friday,
March 23. President H. A. Badger, of the
Fifteenth Ward Relief Society, accompanied
the writer to Nephi and participated in the
business of the meeting; also bore a good
testimony of the Gospel. Sister Mary
Pitchforth has been a model president, but
is at present in very delicate health.
Another visitor at the conference was
President M. A. Hyde, of Sanpete Stake
Relief Society, who bore a strong testimony
concerning Joseph Smith and the Gospel
dispensation.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


R. S. REPORTS.
		SALT LAKE STAKE.

	The Relief Society Conference of the Salt
Lake Stake, held March 3, 1900, in the
Assembly Hall, President M. Isabella
Horne presiding, commenced by singing
"Arise, O glorious Zion." Prayer by
Counselor Clara C. Cannon. Singing,
"Where the voice of friendship’s heard.”
	The officers present on the stand were
President M. I. Horne, Counselors A. T.
Hyde and C. C. Cannon, President Zina D.
H. Young, President Bathsheba W. Smith,
Maria W. Wilcox, Julia C. Howe, Clar-
issa S. Williams, President Emma S.
Woodruff, President Sarah J. Cannon,
Emma A. Empey, Elizabeth J. Stevenson,
President Mary A. Hyde.
	Roll call, twenty-three wards represented
out of twenty-five. Minutes of the confer-
ence held December 23, read and approved.
	President Horne was the first speaker.
Said this was the first meeting held since
the division of the Stake, and she was
pleased to see so good a representation. It
is a grand and a great calling to be an officer
in the Relief Society, and the officers should
be alive to their duties, energetic and faith-
ful in every particular. We must not be
slothful servants, nor let anything interfere
with our spiritual advancement. We need
a revival of interest in our associations, and


[Column 1]
much depends upon the energy of the
officers. All should be humble and willing
to be dictated by the Holy Spirit. Do not
aspire to office, but be willing to fill the
humble places, for they are all important.
All the officers need our prayers.
	President Zina D. H. Young said: “It is
a great thing to be satisfied with the provi-
dences of the Lord, and do not always be
wanting, or when you come to the end of
your lives and are weighed in the balance,
you may still be found wanting. I pray
the Lord to bless all who are here, they are
His jewels, and I also pray for those who
are not here but who desire so much to be
here, if it were possible.”
	President Sarah J. Cannon said: “I feel
that there is a good spirit here, and am very
much pleased to be able to be here. I feel
the importance of what Sister Horne had
said—that we need an awakening in the
Relief Society. I have thought of this a
great deal, and wonder what can be done in
this regard. Be charitable and try to avoid
giving offence to any one.”
	Counselor Annie T. Hyde was extremely
gratified to see the good representation con-
sidering the division of the Stake. Felt
that this division would be productive of
good. Spoke of the duties of mothers, there
is none greater on earth, and the Relief
Society is one of the greatest helps in this
direction. “We hope to have some special
meetings where these things may be con-
sidered. Relief Society workers when they
go around making their visits should invite
all the sisters to come to meeting, try to
get all to come who possibly can. Do not
find fault with those who are in authority
over us, for that is where Satan will gain
possession of our minds.”
	President Bathsheba W. Smith was de-
lighted to meet with the sisters always, and
never regretted coming to a meeting. Dis-
couraged card playing, never saw any good
come from it. “Always be cheerful and
have a contented mind and happy counte-
nance.” Believed that we could make our
meetings more interesting if we would meet
together often and each one strive to do her
duty.
	President M. A. Hyde, of Sanpete Stake,
was delighted to be present and hear the
good instructions given by the sisters. Had
the pleasure of knowing the Prophet Joseph
Smith, and said there were not many left
who had had the privilege of seeing him.
Bore a strong testimony and prayed the
blessing of the Lord to be with all, especial-
ly those who are placed over us to instruct
us.
	President Emma S. Woodruff, of the new
Granite Stake, said they felt in this new
Stake that the Salt Lake Stake was their
mother. “We feel satisfied with the pres-
ent arrangements, though it is a great re-
sponsibility. I feel like my greatest desire
is to serve the Lord for the rest of my life
When our children are young they need
our care, and even when they are grown
we should not slacken our vigilance, for
they need our care almost as much then.”
	Presided Horne explained that on ac-
count of the unavoidable absence of Sister
Riter, Sister Emma A. Empey had kindly
consented to fill her place. Gave instruc-
tions as to how the nurses should report,
etc. It is quite a trouble and care to look
after the sick, but this is a part of our labors
and we should not shirk them.
	Sister Horne introduced Sister Empey,
who spoke for a few minutes, expressing
her desire to give satisfaction in this posi-

[Column 2]
tion, although she hardly knew what her
duties would be.
	Sister Elizabeth Stevenson said: “We
should live lives of example. Look at the
lives of these our sisters, two of them
eighty years old and over. Let the officers
take the instructions home to their branches
of the society. Let us watch well over our
children, they are our jewels. The sisters
should try to assist their husbands, be care-
ful in financial affairs. What a sad thing it
is to see a Latter-day Saint become a bank-
rupt.”
	Meeting adjourned until 2 p. m. Sing-
ing, We thank Thee, O God, for a prophet.”
Benediction by President Elizabeth Paul.
	Afternoon session commenced by singing
“Do what is right,” led by the Temple
choir. Opening prayer by Sister Julia C.
Howe. Singing, “Jesus I my cross have
taken.”
	The minutes of the afternoon session of
the conference held December 23 were read
and approved. The annual financial and
statistical report was read and approved,
also the Stake treasurer’s report read and ap-
proved.
	President Horne was pleased to see a bet-
ter attendance, and urged all the sisters in
the Stake to make an effort to attend. The
influence of the example of the mother was
very hard to eradicate from the minds of
the children. The Prophet said that a
good and pure woman was a fit companion
for angels and her influence over her asso-
ciates was unbounded. Spoke of the neces-
sity of being humble. A humble spirit has
more influence over the people than the
words of great orators. Our trials and
temptations only serve as means of prov-
ing us. We will get our reward for the
good we do and the faith we have. Testi-
fied of the following occurrence which hap-
pened in Canada, at the home of a Mrs.
Walton, near Toronto. Those present were
John Taylor and wife, John Snider and
wife, Joseph Fielding and two sisters,
Joseph Horne and wife and some others.
The Prophet related with great force and
power the circumstances connected with the
bringing forth of the Book of Mormon. His
face and whole body seemed full of light.
Advised the sisters to learn as much as pos-
sible about the Church, and also endeavor
to keep in touch with what is going on in
the world. Prayed for the spirit of the
Lord to bless and prompt us to do right,
and to encourage and cheer us continually.
	Mrs. Priscilla J. Riter, superintendent of
the nurses, spoke of her inability to con-
tinue the work, because of sickness in her
family. Commended those nurses who had
fulfilled every duty required of them in
caring for the poor and sick. Said those
who are most prompt to answer the calls of
charity are the best nurses and are compe-
tent to receive the best wages. Mrs. Riter
spoke at some length in a very interesting
way of the work performed and the spirit
manifested by the nurses. Hoped they
would receive encouragement from the
Society officers, and desired always the sup-
port of the sisters and the blessings of her
Heavenly Father.
	Counselor Annie T. Hyde moved that a
vote of thanks be given Sister Riter for her
very efficient work, and hoped she would
soon be able to take it up again; carried.
	Sister Emmeline B. Wells then spoke of
the 17th of March, the day on which the
Relief Society was organized, and hoped
each ward would celebrate the day in such
a way that an impression would be made on

[Column 3]
the minds of the people in the ward. Make
it a day long to be remembered. She was
very much interested in all work which
would make the Relief Society the best and
most efficient society for the development of
women on the earth. The sisters will never
regret the work they do in the Relief Soci
ety. Referred to the silk industry in Utah.
Everything is favorable to the raising of
silk, but there was not enough cocoons
raised to warrant an experiment station.
The silk commission will assist any one
who wishes to learn about the work. The
Utah silk commission, of which President
Zina D. H. Young was chairman, sent an
elegant black brocaded silk dress, made of
Utah silk, to Miss Anthony as a birthday
present on her eightieth birthday anniver-
versary. Sister Wells spoke of the EX-
PONENT as the organ of the Relief Society.
Said it always contained reports of the Re-
lief Society of the various Stakes of Zion,
and news of interest educational to women.
She had been requested to prepare a com-
plete file for the Paris Exposition, and ex-
pected to do so, with suitable bindings for
that great occcasion. Urged the
sisters to support the paper, it was a
woman’s paper for women. Spoke of hav-
ing represented the Relief Society in the
International Congress in London last sum-
mer. Hoped the sisters would do all they
could to forward the work of the society
We have much information to give
to strangers and we should prepare our-
selves.
	President Zina D. H. Young requested
that every president should have a copy of
the EXPONENT. The Lord will eventually
open the way for the furtherance of His
work. Prayed for the blessings of the
Lord continually to accompany His daugh-
ters.
	President Horne wished the wards to
celebrate the 17th of March. “Officers of
the Salt Lake Stake will meet in the 14th
ward hall four weeks from to day.”
	The Temple choir sang “O, Paradise,”
and the conference was adjourned for three
months, or the first Saturday in June.
Closing prayer by Elder C. J. Thomas.
		AUGUSTA W. GRANT, Sec.
LOS ANGELOS.
	Our little branch at Los Angelos is really
not so large as it has been, owing to many
leaving for Utah, Idaho and other places.
Still, some have been added to the Church
through the labors of our faithful Elders in
this mission.
	Our Relief Society, though not making
so much headway as we could desire, is not
asleep, by any means.
	The president of our branch, Elder H.
C. Jacobson, thought proper some two
weeks since to have a social gathering of
Saints and friends, hiring a large hall for
this purpose. An admission fee was
charged, which proved a success. We took
advantage of this opportunity to offer needle
work for sale, made by the sisters, which
we soon disposed of, realizing some ten dol-
lars. We have one or two friends who are
needy and must do what we can to help
them. We felt pleased with our work, and
all enjoyed themselves, friends as well as
Saints.
	I wish to say my address will again be
531 Court St., which please note. Wish-
ing the compliments of the season, I re-
main,
		Your sister in the Gospel,
				E. WOOLLACOTT.

RELIEF SOCIETY JORDAN STAKE. 
	At a conference held at East Jordan,
January 21, 1900, for the purpose of or-
ganizing the Jordan Stake of Zion, it was
proposed that the following officers of the
Relief Society of the Jordan Stake be
sustained: Marinda Bateman, president;
Hedvig Huldah Larson, first counselor;
Agnes Cutler, second counselor. 
	The appointments were sustained unani-
mously, and Marinda Bateman was set
apart January 27, 1900, by Francis M.
Lyman; Hedvig H. Larson, same date, by
Anton H. Lund; Agnes Cutler, by Francis
M. Lyman; Elizabeth Gardner, chosen as
secretary on February 10, 1900, set apart
on the same date by Francis M. Lyman;
Emily White, chosen as corresponding
secretary on February 10, set apart March
10, by Orin P. Miller; Mary Aggie Cushing 
chosen as treasurer on February 10, set
apart March 16 by Hyrum Goff. 
			ELIZABETH GARDNER, Sec.


FAREWELL SURPRISE PARTY.
	Though it is for the benefit of the people
that the new wards are being organized, it
causes a feeling of regret and sadness to
part with some of the members who are
thus moved to start in the fresh field in
which they are called to labor.
	It was with such feelings that the mem-
bers of Willow Creek Ward met on the 1st
of March to give a farewell party to Sister
Thompson, who besides being an officer of
the Stake Y. L. M. I. A., was president of
the same association of the ward, and a
teacher in our Relief Society, and was also
secretary of the Sunday School.
	In a neat little speech Sister Ida Barratt,
in behalf of these associations, presented
our sister with a wreath of flowers, made
from the hair of the members of the afore-
said associations, as a token of respect and
appreciation of her labors among us.
	In response Sister Thompson said she
was sure the ward had been united and suc-
cessful in getting up this surprise, and in
their efforts of showing their appreciation
of her labors, and that enough money could
not be laid at her feet to buy this wreath of
flowers that had so affectionately been pre-
sented to her.
	After a continuation of the program all
went home, feeling that they had done
their duty, and all felt happy. 
					LITTLE SISTER.


RESOLUTIONS OF RESPECT.
	To the memory of Sister Julia Anna Ivins
		Pace, who departed this life Jan. 17, 1900.
	Whereas, In the dispensation of Providence,
God has seen fit to call home one of his daughters
who labored long as corresponding secretary for
the Relief Society of the St. George Stake of Zion;
and
	Whereas, she has proved herself an efficien
worker in all her various duties, and was one
whose kindness and generosity of spirit brought
sunshine to many hearts, helping the needy in
time of want, and was in her own life a fitting em-
bodiment of her constant prayer, that she might be
humble and united in all that is good; therefore,
be it
	Resolved, That we, her sisters, united with
her in the presidency of the St. George Stake, do
deeply feel her loss in connection with her earnest
effort and constant work; nevertheless, in this our
loss, we feel to bow to the mandates of an Alwise
Creator who doeth all things well.
	Resolved, that a copy of these resolutions be
sent to her family, also that one be sent to the
WOMAN’S EXPONENT for publication. 
			ANN C. WOODBURY, President.
			EMMA MORRIS, First Counselor.
			JANE F. BLEAK, Second Counselor.


OBITUARY.
LUCY H. FLAKE.
	Died, in Snowflake, Arizona, January 27, 1900, 
Sister Lucy H. Flake, wife of William J. Flake, 
one of the Bishopric of this ward. 
	Sister Lucy Hannah White Flake was the eldest 
child of Samuel and Mary Burton White. She 
was born in Knox Co., Ill., August 23, 1842. Her
parents came to Utah in 1850, and were among 
the first settlers of Lehi, moving from there to
Cedar in 1853. She was married to William
J. Flake, December 30, 1858, by Apostle Amasa
M. Lyman, in Cedar City, Utah. She was the 
mother of thirteen children, nine sons and four
daughters, six of whom, five sons and one
daughter, have preceded her to the other side. 
	With her husband and family she left their home
in Beaver, where they had lived nearly eighteen 
years, and came to Arizona in the fall of 1877. 
They lived for awhile on the Little Colorado river, 
but not being able to make a dam stay in the 
river, Brother Flake bought the home and ranch
of Mr. James Stinson, on Silver creek, in July, 
1878, from which the town of Snowflake was made, 
which has been the home of Sister Flake ever since. 
	She was sustained as first counselor in our Relief
Society to Mary J. West, president, when it was
first organized, July 12, 1880. She acted in that
capacity for fifteen and a half years, when she
was sustained as Stake president of the Primary
Association in November, 1895, in which position
she gained the love and respect of the children as
well as the parents, by making regular and fre-
quent visits to the different branches in the Stake.
She has been head teacher in the primary depart-
ment of our Sunday School for five years, where 
she has been very efficient and faithful.
	Sister Flake was taken sick on the 10th of De-
cember, 1899, with an affliction of the bowels, and 
suffered very much for two weeks, then so far re-
covered as to be able to go to the Primary Christ-
mas tree, Old Folks’ Day and other places during 
the holiday week, but on the 2nd of January she
was again taken down, suffering very severely 
and not eating a meal from then till the end came. 
	All that could be done by husband, children and
loving friends was done for her, but all to no pur-
pose. Her son, Osmer D. Flake, is on a mission, 
and is now president of the Mississippi Conference. 
Her second daughter, Jane F. Wood, lives in
Mexico. The other two daughters and three sons
were at her bedside. 
	She has been a faithful Latter-day Saint, a true
wife and mother, a wise counselor and friend, a 
prompt tithe-payer, has kept the Word of Wisdom
and has been a constant subscriber to the
EXPONENT, only missing the year they moved to 
Arizona of subscribing every year. 
	As members of the Relief Society to which she 
was so faithful, we deeply deplore her loss and 
sympathize with her husband and children, and 
pray that we may emulate her honest, upright
course in life, that our reward may be as sure as 
we feel dear Sister Lucy H. Flake’s will be.
			MAY H. LARSON, 
		President Snowflake R. S.


GEORGE WASHINGTON ASSOCIATION.

The George Washington Memorial Asso-
ciation held its annual meeting at Washing.
ton, D. C., December 13, 14 and 15, 1899.
There was a large attendance from the
several organized States and much interest
was felt by those who could not be present,
as the voting of several hundred proxies
showed. The first session was devoted to
reports of officers,and the annual election of
new officers—Mrs. L. D. M. Sweat, of Port-
land, Me., president of the Mt. Vernon
Association, was chosen-president by accla-
mation. The other officers are as follows:

VICE-PRESIDENTS.
Mrs. Archibald Hopkins, Washington;
Mrs. Washington A. Roebling, New Jersey;
Mrs. Henry R. Mallory, New York; Mrs.
Frank Northrop, New York; Mrs. Law-
rence Maxwell, Jr., Ohio: Mrs. Elizabeth
T.King, Maryland; Mrs. Tod Helmuth,
New York.

TRUSTEES.
Mrs. Phoebe. A. Hearst, California; Mrs.
Calvin B. Brice, Ohio; Mrs. H. H. Adams,
Connecticut; Mrs. Wm. T. Carter, Pennsyl-
vania; Mrs. John L. Routt, Colorado; Mrs.
Leslie C. Wead, Massachusetts.
Recording Secretary of Association—
Mrs. Susanna P. Gage, Ithaea, N. Y.
Recording Secretary of Board—Mrs.
Leslie C. Wead, Brookline, Mass.
Corresponding Secretary—Mrs. John
Russell Young. Washington, D. C.
In the opening address the president, Mrs. Ellen A. Richardson, said: “We
seek on this occasion to promote the final
and supreme, object which Washington
sought to secure in the last hours of his
life. However much confused the work-
ings of this association may become on ac-
count of the long postponed governmental
action and defeated legislation, we never-
theless can but anticipate the happy day
when the last purpose of Washington shall
be in some manner fulfilled. The fulfill-
ment may not come in the very terms which
he prescribed, but fortune and Providence
may yet combine to make such a fulfillment
as shall demonstrate the loyalty of the
American people to the memory of their
first and greatest citizen.”
Greetings were read from Mrs. Henry
Sanger Snow, president of the D. R. Soci-
ety; Mrs. Daniel Manning, president of the
D.A. R. Society Mrs. W. B. Lowe, presi-
dent of the General Federation of Women’s
Clubs, and others.
The report of the treasurer, Mrs. Frank
Northrop, showed an addition of $6,000 to
the contingent and building funds, and
pledges of $8,000 to the building fund. Four
hundred and eighty-two members have
been admitted during the year, making a
total membership of over fourteen thousand.
Reports of State Chairmen and Questions
of Policy occupied the second day’s session,
which adjourned at 4 p. m., to allow the
members to attend the memorial celebra-
tion in Epiphany Church. The charter
membership closed at noon, December 14,
1898, and many distinguished names were
added to the members, among them, Hon.
James Wilson, Secretary of Agriculture;
Dr. Dabney, President of the University of
Tennessee; Mrs. Ellen Hardin Walworth,
Saratoga, N. Y., and Mr. Charles D. Wal-
cott, Director U. S. Geological Survey.
Letters were read from Dr. Benjamin
Ide Wheeler, President of the University
of California, and Dr. Shurman, President
of Cornell University, and a member of the
Education Committee, endorsing the estab-
lishment of a national university, and re-
marks were made to this end by Dr. Dab-
ney, Mr. Walcott and Dr. W. J. McGee,
Chief of the Bureau of Ethnology, all em-
phasizing the purpose and object of the
association to build a Memorial Building,
which shall be devoted to such educational
uses as shall fulfill, as far as possible, the
wishes of Washington, as the policy of the
Association.
In the evening a solemn observance of
the centennial of Washington’s death was
held in the Lafayette Opera House. This
commemoration was by the women of the
land through the George Washington Mem-
orial Association, which, some three years
ago, suggested that there be some national
observance of the day in Washington. The
celebration was of the most solemn and im-
pressive character and filled the Opera
House with a large audience of representa-
tive men and women. In the boxes were
representatives of the Cabinet, Diplomatic
Corps and Supreme Court, and in the seats


heads of colleges, government officials, rep-
resentatives of different creeds and men of
different political minds—all there to testify
to the universal love and esteem in which
Washington is held today.
	The theatre was decorated with flags, tall
palms and other potted plants. A portrait
of Washington and the President’s flag
were suspended over the heads of the
speakers. Over a hundred members of the
Washington Choral Union occupied the
rear of the platform, singing “Before Jeho-
vah’s Mighty Throne” and the Hallelujah
Chorus, with telling effect. The instru-
mental music was furnished by the Fourth
Artillery band, which was present by
special order of the secretary of War. After
the rendition of Chopin’s Funeral March
and another dead march, “At Rest,” fre-
quently played at military funerals, the
exercises were opened by the Right Rev.
Henry Y. Satterlee, Bishop of Washington,
who offered a fervent prayer, invoking the
Creator to instil into the American people
the national spirit which so strongly char-
acterized the life of Washington.
	Hon. John A. Kasson, former minister
to Austria and Germany, acting as Master
of Ceremonies, reading an original letter of
Washington’s and speaking briefly of his
will, in which he devoted part of his for-
tune to the establishment of a national
University. He said in closing, “Wash-
ington proposed no elaborate mausoleum
for himself, no monument to his own great-
ness, no memorial bridges, but his great
heart and intellect did desire a monument
of good, not formed of marble, but of loving
stones, a National University into which
advanced students might enter from every
section of the land, and remove sectional
lines and local prejudices. His chief wish
has remained unfilled for a hundred years.
It is singularly appropriate that the noble
womanhood of the land should at last
undertake the fulfillment of his last desire.”
	Dr. W. J. McGee read the extract from
Washington’s will bequeathing to Congress
fifty shares of the Potomac Company to be
used for the establishment of a National
University.
	Dr. P. B. Barringer, President of the
University of Virginia, gave an account of
Washington’s death, Dr. J. L. Curry,
former Minister to Spain, spoke at consider-
able length on “Washington as a framer of
the Constitution,” and after music by the
Choral Union Dr. Chas. W. Dabney, Resi-
dent of the University of Tennessee, made
the closing address. He referred to Wash-
ington as our first great expansionist, early
seeking to extend our government over the
western country and bind all its people to
us. “Everything he did,” the speaker
said, “from the forming of the Constitution,
which was to bind the states together, to
the construction of inland lines of naviga-
tion, which were to bind them together in
commerce, had for its object the develop-
ment of the national spirit—all his plans
were shaped to this end, that America, the
land of the free, should become a great
nation.”
	A letter was read from Cardinal Gibbons,
praising Washington’s toleration of religion,
and the ceremonies concluded with “The
Star Spangled Banner.”
	The last session, December 15, was de-
voted to amending the by-laws, and new
business, and at its close the members sep-
arated, feeling that the Association was
nearer the attainment of its object than
ever before.		KATE H. WARD.

RELIEF SOCIETY GRANITE STAKE.
	The people who attended the conference
at Mill Creek ward were honored with the
presence and wise instructions of Apostle
Francis M. Lyman, Anton H. Lund,
Abraham Owen Woodruff, Seymour B.
Young, of the Seven Presidents of the
Seventies, and others of the authorities of
the Church January 28, 1900, for the pur-
pose of organizing the Granite Stake of
Zion.
	Sister Emma Woodruff was chosen Stake
president of the Relief Society, and Sisters
Mary P. Young and Katharine H. Brock-
bank, as counselors. President Emma
Woodruff was set apart in the afternoon by
Apostle Anton H. Lund.
	The following Saturday at Farmer’s
Ward, February 3, 1900, the counselors
were set apart by Apostle Francis M. Ly-
man, Abraham Owen Woodruff, Bishop
John R. Winder and President Frank Y.
Taylor. Sister Celestia A. S. Young was
chosen and set apart as secretary, and Maria
B. Winder, assistant secretary and treasurer
both, until the other office could be filled.
Celestia. A. S. Young, Sec.


NOTES.
	MRS. LUCINDA H. STONE passed away
at her home in Kalamazoo, Michigan, on
March 14, aged eighty-six yerrs.
	MRS. HAMMOND, of Swaffham Manor,
Norfolk, England, who died recently at
the age of ninety-four, was the daughter
of Mary Charworth, whom Byron loved so
hopelessly. Miss Charworth married Mr.
Muster, who took the name of his wife.
	QUEEN WILHELMINA, of Holland, is
said to be looking forward with appre-
hension to the visit of the Shah of Persia,
who is expected to pay his respects to her
as he passes through Holland on his way
to the Paris Exposition. When the late
Shah visited Antwerp, the king’s palace
was put at his disposal, and after his de-
parture the government was obliged to
renovate the carpets, upholstery and a
great deal of the furniture.
