TO MRS. M. ISABELLA HORNE. 
A BIRTHDAY GBEETING.
 
A life replete with brave and noble deeds,
Wrought in sweet patience and humility,
With loving thought for all humanity, 
And that which ev’ry living creature needs.

Eighty and one, long years, how strange it seems,
That you should see so many wond’rous things,
As recollection to your memory brings;
Ti at Israel’s Prophets only saw in dreams,

Ah, me ! what ’tis to live so many years,
And toil and labor, all the time for good,
Thro’ youth and wedded life, and widowhood,
Such shifting scenes of hope, of joy, and fears.

Courageous woman, you have earn’d a rest;
Wisely you’ve striven for ‘‘the better part;”
And trials borne that pierced you to the heart,
‘‘Tried as by fire,” yet bravely stood the test.

O, may the years to come, be full of love,
Of children and of children’s children, too,
And rich in evr’y blessing dear and true,
Foretaste of that celestial world above.

And when we meet upon ‘‘the other side ”
Then may we know each other’s faces well,
And gladly greeting, all our vict’ries tell,
Where peace and truth forevermore abide.

There may your lov’d ones who have gone before
Salute you with the songs of yesterday,
You’ve sung together on life’s glad highway,
Sweet murm ring music echoed o’er and o’er.
Emmeline. B. Wells.
Nov. 20, 1899.


MRS. ZINA D. H. YOUNG.
AND THE RELIEF SOCIETY.
Mrs. Zina D. H. Young, lovingly known
by many thousands of people as “Aunt
Zina,” has had a most remarkable life his-
tory. Being one of the early Church
members, she has known the mobbings
and drivings, plundering and persecution of
various kinds experienced in this age of
the world only by the people of whose
religion she has been ever a most zealous
and a leading member. But it is of that
part of her life, (and that is a considerable
portion) which has been spent in Relief
Society work to which I will now refer.
It was shortly after the first organization
of the Relief Society, effected by the
Prophet Joseph Smith in Nauvoo, and in-
cluding but nineteen members, that Aunt
Zina became a member. She was then a
young woman about twenty-one years of
age. Being now seventy-nine, a long life
has been employed in the exercise of

[Column 2]
charity, that grace denominated by St.
Paul as the greatest of all.
The expulsion of the people from Nauvoo
made it impossible for the Relief Society to
continue operating long as an organized
body. But that very event, by the sick-
ness, suffering and sorrow entailed, made
greater the demands upon the sisters for
charity in acts of love and kindness.
Not long after the settlement of the
Saints in Salt Lake valley, the Relief
Society was organized “as a natural out-
growth of the parent society in Nauvoo.”
It was the desire of President Young that
Miss Eliza R. Snow assist in forming the
organizations with Mrs. Zina D. H. Young
as her counselor. The Relief Society
branches have increased until now they
exist in all of the forty-two Stakes of Zion,
in outside states and in foreign lands,
wherever are located enough members of
the Church to organize one. At one time
there were thirty branches of the Relief
Society on the Sandwich Islands; the num-
ber is now probably twenty-nine, emigra-
tion causes some changes. There are
some in Germany and Switzerland, also in
Norway, Sweden, Denmark, England,
Scotland, Wales and Ireland. In the
United States they exist in Kansas City,
Chicago, the states of Washington, Oregon,
California, Nevada, Idaho and Arizona.
And in Canada and Mexico there are or-
ganized Stakes. Each Stake has its set of
Relief Society officers complete, and these
are presided over by the general board.
Aunt Zina has lived to see the Relief
Society grow from that one organization in
Nauvoo to six hundred branches, with a
membership of over thirty thousand. She has
been present at and assisted in the organ-
ization of very many of these, traveling in
heat or cold, sunshine or storm, comfort or
discomfort, as the circumstances allowed.
And when not so engaged she has spent
much of her time in visiting different Stakes
and organizations, giving instruction and
counsel, ministering unto the sick, com-
forting and inspiring to good works all with
whom she came in contact by the blessed
influence of her heavenly spirit. Besides
all the other work she has done, she has
presided innumerable times at the impor-
tant event of incoming life. And none can
say how many times she has been present
to bless and to comfort the sick and the
dying. With patience and long-suffering
has she listened to the plaints of poor
humanity, ministering in either a spiritual
or temporal way. For many years she
was chief nurse in the family of President
Young. Often and often she has been
called an “angel of mercy.” Viewing the
work she has done, one is led to think she
had a special mission to fill in this life, per-
haps appointed unto her “before the
foundation of the earth.”
After the death of the president, Eliza R.
Snow in December, 1887, the general board
was reorganized with Zina D. H. Young as
president, a position she has continued to
fill with dignity and honor to the present
time.
Under the auspices of the Relief Society

[Column 3]
a great effort has been made to establish
the silk industry in Utah. In the year
1857 President Brigham Young sent for
silk worm eggs, and, at his request, Aunt
Zina commenced the raising and care of the worms, beginning June 1, 1869, at
Forest farm, now known as Forest Dale.
She was the pioneer in that line of work in
our State, and was made president of the
Silk Association, organized later. She and
her aids traveled the length and breadth of
the territory, using the most earnest efforts
to get the industry well established.
Another great work done by the Relief
Society was the organization of the Deseret
Hospital Association, on May 12, 1882.
Eliza R. Snow was president, with Zina D.
H. Young vice-president, and a full corps
of officers. “The Relief Society Stake
presidents were members of the honorary
committee, and were expected to represent,
the interests of the hospital in their respec-
tive localities.” After years of toil and
struggle the hospital was closed.
In all of these labors Aunt Zina has been
one of the foremost workers. But the
general public cannot realize the beauty of
her home life. I heard one of her children
say she had never known her mother to
utter an impatient word at the trials and
difficulties besetting her. She took them
as lessons intended for her by the Great
Father. And one may say she has gradu-
ated with honor from the school of patient
endurance and self-sacrifice. Man has his
special fields of labor and woman hers. Aunt Zina has done what it would have
been impossible for man to accomplish.
Her spirit is one of the meekest of the earth
and yet one of the most majestic. Presi-
dent Young paid high tributes to her
qualities as a mother. One of her admir-
ers penned the following lines, which it
seems appropriate to quote:
“In little ways unknown to a man  Aunt Zina oft has raised her hand, To soothe the heart, the swollen eye,
When none but she and God were nigh. She’s traveled o’er the dusty plain,
The desert hot in hail and rain,
And talked where brethren could not.”
A sketch of Aunt Zina’s life necessarily
includes much of the work done by the
society, but not all. The charity work
that has been done, in the way of feeding
and clothing the poor, making quilts to
comfort them through the inclement season,
looking after the sick, dying and dead,
alleviating the sorrows of the distressed—of
this work all can never be told. Besides
this, lands have been bought and halls
erected for the use of the society. When
the society became national and inter-
national some advantages were gained.
The president of the general board and a
delegate have the privilege of represent-
ing the society at the National Council of
Women. In that way an idea of the great
work done by the Relief Society organiz-
ation is given to women from all parts of
the world. Aunt Zina and others have
gone to represent us at these councils.
The most recent move of great impor-
tance is the organization of the nurses


class. In the Salt Lake Stake women re-
ceive instruction free, Dr. Margaret C. Roberts
instructor, and in return, after their train-
ing, they give their services free for a cer-
tain time, subject to the call of the superin-
tendent, Mrs. Priscilla J. Riter.
The next thing greatly to, be desired is
to awaken an active interest in the younger
women of the Church, particularly those
who by natural endowment or superior edu-
cational advantages could help give an im-
petus to the work. Perhaps then the much
desired central building in Salt Lake City
could become a reality. A building where
all the important records of the Relief
Society could be kept, a library maintained
and perhaps sleeping apartments and din-
ing room for the entertainment of distant
visitors, an assembly room, recreation hall,
and bureau of information.
Perhaps, too, as the work becomes en-
larged, the poor will be so well cared for
by establishing industries and giving em-
ployment to the able and caring for the
unable, that real poverty would scarcely be
known.
Perhaps, too, rooms can be found, and,
what is more, just the right teachers to
gather the children from the streets in the
evening, affording them attractive enter-
tainment and profitable instruction, which
might be the means of saving many
precious souls. The great and admirable
work that has been done can be recorded,
but the work that may in the future be
done, only a prophet can fortell. Anything
in the way of good and charitable work
done in an organized way may be imagined
and perhaps become a reality.
E. S. WILCOX.

WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


SIXTH WARD ANNIVERSARY.

The second anniversary of the Sixth Ward
Relief Society of this city, held in com-
memoration of the first organization by our
beloved prophet, Joseph Smith, in Nauvoo,
was held in the Sixth Ward Assembly
Hall, March 17, 1900, President West pre-
siding, Counselors Ellen R.Watson and
Alice Butterworth assisting. On the stand
were Bishop Jas. C. Watson, Counselor
Jesse West, Brothers Alexander Burt,
Edwin Rushton, Thomas Green, James
Poulton, Sen., David Fullmer, Thomas
Winter, Carl A. Ek and George Irvine.
Meeting opened by singing “We thank
Thee, O God, for a prophet,” organist,
Sisters Louisa Giuique and Sister Jane
Poulton. Prayer by Brother Edwin
Rushton.
A short sketch of Relief Society work
was read by the secretary.
President West in her opening remarks
gave a warm welcome to the brethren and
sisters present. Spoke of the good spirit
here today and the surprise awaiting her.
“In looking over this clean hall I cannot
express fully my gratitude to my young
sisters, also Counselor Arnold G. Giuique,
who has been untiring in assisting to reno-
vate this hall, and to all who aided in this
work.” Regretted that her health was
such that she was too feeble to aid in this.
The Lord would bless all who were valiant
in the cause of truth.
Bishop James C. Watson felt honored in
being connected in this noble work with
the sisters of the Relief Society. Approved
of the sketch read by the secretary. “The
financial report speaks well for the good
work the sisters are performing.” In be-
half of the brethren and sisters, returned
thanks to those who had so ably renovated
the hall, the young sisters also to the sis-
ters of this society who have kindly donated
carpet for the stand; and the choir, not for-
getting Brother and Sister Butterworth,
who kindly donated two table spreads and
a mat for the stand. Regretted the absence
of Counselor Arnold G. Giuique, who is
very sick this afternoon, through overwork
in his zeal to have everything done in
order. “In every capacity we find him ever
faithful. May the blessings of our heavenly
Father rest on him and all who are faithful
workers in the church and kingdom of
God. The spirit of peace is here to day,
everything is pleasant to look upon; the
sisters of this society are in harmony with
the Bishopric, they are noble women doing
a grand work assisting the poor and needy.
President West is heart and soul in this
work.” He could more fully understand
now why President Young adopted as an
emblem the hive of Deseret, for we are like
the busy bees, earnest workers. Invoked 
the blessings of the Lord on those present.
Counselor Jesse West expressed his
pleasure in meeting with the brethren and
sisters on this occasion also in hearing the
report, the sisters were engaged in a good
work. Felt to thank the Lord that his lot
was cast in these valleys with the Saints of
God, where we could go up to the house of
the Lord and perform our work for the
dead. Spoke of the magnitude of this
Latter-day work. The sisters of the Relief
Society were blest and would continue to
be blest. Spoke of the first organization of
the Relief Society—the Prophet Joseph
was inspired.
Brother Alexander Burt said it gave him
pleasure to hear the good report of the sis-
ters, and endorsed the sentiments of the
former speakers. In this comfortable hall
we cannot fail to feel the spirit of peace.
Spoke of the advancement the brethren and
sisters who were faithful to the truth were
making. This Latter-day work embraces
all that is good, the Lord chooses the hum-
ble and meek of the earth to do His work.
Counselor Ellen R. Watson expressed
her pleasure in meeting with the brethren
and sisters. Desired to be one with the
Bishopric and sisters of the society. Spoke
of the good work the teachers were doing.
Their’s is one of the most important auxili-
aries in the Relief Society.
Counselor Alice Butterworth desired to
be one with those who were serving the
Lord. Spoke of the hardships and poverty
existing in early days in these valleys,
some forty years ago the change that
wealth has brought about the indifference
many are exhibiting. The Lord had blest
her with means, desired to use it for the
furtherance of the Gospel. Spoke of the
progression the Church had made since
early days.
Brother Winter said, “one year ago today
we met under similar circumstances. Many
inducements had been offered him to leave
Zion, but he never had any desire to do so.
Felt to stay with the people of God where
he could hear the voice of His servants and
carryout their counsel and instruction. The
day is not far distant when we shall thank
our Heavenly Father for His protecting
care over us in these valleys, for tumult
and strife are abroad in the earth.” Closed
his remarks by invoking the blessing of
the Lord on President West and her aids.
Brother James Poulton, Sen., took
pleasure in being present with this Relief
Society, lie was in full fellowship with
them and desired to be known as such.
Felt we were having a feast. Many re-
flections had passed through his mind this
afternoon, and he felt the peaceful influence
prevading here. Spoke of the perfect sys-
tem existing in the Church. The Relief
Society was one of the greatest auxiliaries
in it, the sisters had, and were, doing a
great work. The blessing of the Lord at-
tended their labors. Spoke of the benevo-
lence of Queen Victoria and her daughters,
who were performing charitable acts among
the poor, making clothing and sending
things to those in need. Many of the nobles
of the earth were aiding the poor and
needy, for this they would receive a bless-
ing. The Relief Society were more succsss-
ful. They have obeyed the truth and God
has given them power to minister to the
needy and comfort and bless the sick. Let
us give the honor and glory to our Heaven-
ly Father for these blessings. Felt to ask
God to bless all those who were workers in
this great cause, even the Relief Society.
Prayed for President West, that the Lord
would strengthen her for her integrity to
the Gospel.
Brother David Fullmer was pleased with
the good records the sisters were making,
the aids they are to the Bishopric. Said
God bless them for their noble work.
Spoke of the surprise he felt on entering
this room, the young sisters have done a
good work, we appreciate their labors.
Brother Thomas Green appreciated the
honor in being invited on this occasion, not
alone for the feast prepared, but to hear
the good instruction given here today.
Spoke of the integrity of President West
and her untiring devotion to the sick.
Prayed the Lord to bless her future days
with health and strength.
Brother Carl A. Ek said, “My heart is
full of thanksgiving to my Heavenly
Father for revealing the Gospel in these
last days.” Felt that the sisters of the
Relief Society were blest in their labors.
Spoke of the respect he felt for the aged,
who had grown old in the service of the
Lord; who had borne the burden and heat
of the day. Alluded to the privilege of
baptizing his mother, she being the first
one he baptized after he embraced the Gos-
pel, also had the satisfaction of seeing her
die in full faith of the Gospel.
The Bishop, in his closing remarks, re
referred to those who did not honor their
calling, who were absent from meeting,
neglected their duties and were not, willing
to fill their positions such should be re-
leased. For those who had small children
he had charity, and felt to bless them and
urge them to care for them. But for those
who wilfully neglected their duties he had
no sympathy. The Lord would bless the
sisters who were faithful and make their
burdens light.
Singing, “Do what is right.” Benedic-
diction by Brother George Irvine.
Refreshments having been previously
prepared by the sisters, the brethren and
sisters partook of the same. After a social
chat all retired feeling greatly blest and
benefited.

ELIZABETH W. CONDIE, Sec.


Miss Susan B. Anthony, and Mrs.
Carrie Chapman Catt will both speak at the
annual Festival of the New England
Woman Suffrage Association, to be held in
Boston the last of May. The occasion bids
fair to be even more brilliant than usual.

EMBRYO.
I feel a poem in my heart tonight
	A still thing growing;
As if the darkness to the outer light
	A song were owing;
A something vague, and sweet, and sad;
	Fair, fragile, slender;
Not tearful, yet not daring to be glad,
	And oh! so tender.

It may not reach the outer world at all,
	Despite its growing;
Upon a poet-flower such cold winds fall
	To blight its blowing;
But oh! whatever may the thing betide,
	Free life or fetter,
My heart, just to have held it till it died,
	Will be the better.


LILIES AND VIOLETS.

TO E. J. A.

Lilies of the Valley o’er a grave I see,
Droop their dainty heads in loving sympathy,
And meck-eyed violets, breathing patience, trust,
Shed fragrance on the air, ’bove the sleeping dust.
Crocuses and hyacinths peep through the earth,
Heralding the Springtime’s near approaching birth.
The world’s hum and bustle, seems far, far away,
Falls the solemn stillness of a Sabbath day.

A mother bends in tears, o’er the lowly bed,
While her first-born sleeps, ’mong the uncounted
dead;
Tenderly her hands twine flowers ’mid the leaves,
Touching them so gently, while her spirit grieves.
Again she feels baby hands, close round her
	pressed,
Kisses the tiny head, laid upon her breast;
But, O, ’tis past and 'mid the quivering pain,
The sad refrain is ringing, “never again.”

The wind blows piercing cold, storm-clouds over-
	head,
And the morning’s sunshine, suddenly has fled;
The sea moans in the distance, beautiful sea, 
That soothed him to sleep in smiling infancy.
The years roll backward, though to manhood he
grew,
The past seems the present, and the years but
	few,
Death, how like the sea, hides other lands so fair,
That none e’er may know, till they cross over there.
So leave him with his God, He doeth all things
	well,
Giveth His beloved rest, where angels dwell.

Unto the mourning heart, whispers words of
	peace,
That from the fret of life, bring a sweet release;
Though the chain is broken, golden chain of love,
Yet affection's tendrils reach far up above;
Clinging around the loved, on the unknown shore,
Where they dwell with  Him, in bliss forever more.
Birds are sweetly trilling ’mong the sighing trees.
Songs of exultation, bringing sad hearts ease.

LYDIA D. ALDER.
	St. Lewards-on-sea, Jan. 1900


CURRENT EVENTS
		
			MARCH.

	In the great cities of the world there has
been much of late to study and observe.
The path of progress in America has not
been without its impediments and difficul-
ties, but progress, nevertheless, real and
striking, has stood preeminent. The
Phillipine situation has continued to be the
leading topic of serious discussion with no
point of absolute unanimity, except the
fervent hope and prayer that the pending
warfare between the United States troops
and the natives come to a speedy end. The
Secretary of War has just returned from a 
trip through the island of Cuba, the chief
object of which was to observe the present
conditions there—political as well as mili-
tary. The result of his observations, as
summed up in interviews of the Press, is
highly encouraging. No definite action
has been taken on the Porto Rico Bill, the
Republican Senators have been conferring
over the bill for some time, but have been
unable to come to any agreement in the
matter.
	A most important bill was passed in the
House of Representatives, March 13. It
had been passed by the Senate and has now 
become a law, the President having affixed
his signature March 14. This bill is called
"The Gold Standard Currency Bill."
	Telegrams dated March 13, state that 
Bloemfontein was occupied by the British
troops. President Steyn and the fighting
Burghers of the Orange Free State have 
fled into the Transvaal.
	March 14 reports were circulated that the
government at Washington had tendered its
good offices in behalf of peace between
Great Britain and the South African Re-
public. It is feared that the peace confer-
ence will prove a complete failure, as up to the
present time not one of the powers who 
signed the three articles agreed on has
officially ratified the treaty.
	March 17, more commonly known as St.
Patrick's Day, was enthusiastically cele-
brated throughout Great Britain and her 
colonies.
	The Paris Exposition Buildings are
nearing completion and travel thither has 
already commenced.
	The first edition of Rev. Dr. Shelden's
paper, the Topeka Capital, appeared March
13. Rev. Dr. Shelden experimented and
published a paper which has excited much
comment, favorable and otherwise. The
object of this experiment is to publish a
paper on strictly moral and honest lines.
In commenting on this paper other editors
insist that it is not possible to make a per-
manent success of such a paper as the
Shelden edition, for the reason that the pub-
lic buys its newspapers to read the news,
and that the paper which endeavors to
preach instead of giving the news, will
never be popular with the masses.
	An event still fresh in the minds of all, is
the assassination of Senator Goebel, of
Kentucky. The foul crime is still shrouded
in mystery, but the keenest detectives are
at work and a number of men prominent in
official life have been arrested as accessor-
ies to the crime. The investigation is very
sensational.
	A distinguished visitor was in our midst
during March in the person of Hon.
Wm. J. Bryan, and later still Hon. Tom
Fitch, a famous Republican orator.
	On Saturday, March 10, the remains of
Abraham Lincoln were removed from under
the Lincoln monument at Springfield, Ill.,
and placed in a temporary vault.
	Among the notable deaths of the month
was John Ruskin, an English writer, art
critic and reformer, and Abram I. M. Wise,
of the Hebrew Cincinnati College.
	In concluding this brief sketch of the
month's events, let us join in hoping that
ere another month elapses the white flag of
truce will float over the nations of the
world, and that progress and true civiliza-
tion will continue its onward course.
				Aimee C. Schiller.


ANOTHER PIONEER GONE.

	Died, January 19, 1900, in the sixty-
ninth year of her age, Mary Stone, at her
home in Alameda Co., California. She
was born August 7, 1831, in Sucick Co.,
New Jersey. Here her parents joined the
Church and emigrated to Kirtland, Ohio,
and afterwards to Nauvoo, Ill. She was
married in March, 1846, to John Murdock,


and left with the Saints in the same
spring for Winter Quarters on the Mis-
souri river. Her young husband died in
August, the day before arriving at their
destination, and was the first white man
buried in the Indian mound at Winter
Quarters. This left her a widow at the
age of fifteen. Her father, David Norris,
was killed at Nauvoo on the 16th of
September, the same year, while with
others defending the homes of the Saints
from mob violence. Her mother died in
November, six weeks later, while on her
journey to Winter Quarters.
	Sister Mary Norris Murdock started
with the pioneer companies in the spring
of 1847, for the valley, arriving the latter
part of September. Two years later she
was married to Samuel Holmes and was
one of the first settlers in Kaysville,
known as Holmes ranch, two and a half
miles north of Haight's ranch. While
living there she had seven children born,
and sometime in the sixties she emigrated
with her husban and family to San Fran-
cisco, and from there to Mission San Jose,
where another child was born. Three
months later her husband was accidentally 
killed by the falling of a ladder while topping
out a hay stack. Eight years after, she
married Mr. Stone. She had two children
born to her by this marriage.
	All her journey from New Jersey to her
home in San Francisco was done by teams
in covered wagons. She was of a patient,
hopeful nature, and was beloved for her
many noble qualities. She was ever ready
to respond to the call of any one in sick-
ness, and for many years was found at the
bedside of her neighbors for miles around,
many times going in the night over rough
roads, administering comfort to the suf-
fering. She passed quietly away sur-
rounded by the loving faces of her chil-
dren. Her sister, Louisa Norris Dicker,
who resides in this city, was telegraphed
for but only arrived a few hours after her
sister had breathed her last, which was a
sad disappointment, as they were very
fond of each other.
	Sister Stone had a complimentary ticket
to the pioneer Jubilee, and came here after
thirty-five years absence, meeting many
old friends and making some new ones.
She would often speak of it afterwards as
one of the great pleasures of her life.
						L. N. D.


HONOR TO WHOM HONOR IS DUE.
Last Thursday, March 29, Sister Agnes
Cutler was tendered a very pleasant sur-
prise party at her home in East Jordan.
For the past three years Sister Cutler has
been the active president of the ward Re-
lief Society, but with the organization of
the Jordan Stake, she was chosen second
counselor to the Stake president of the Re-
lief Society, Sister Marinda Bateman.
The surprise party was given to show
the respect and appreciation in which Sister
Cutler is held, and to wish her God-speed
in her more exalted calling. The time
was spent enjoyably, singing, reciting and
partaking of the bounties of this life, after
which Sister Cutler was presented with a
handsome present, consisting of a piece of
household furniture. Over seventy Relief
Society workers were present, among the
more prominent being Sisters Marinda
Bateman and Hilda Larsen, of the Stake
presidency, and Sister Emily White, assis-
tant Stake secretary.

SALT LAKE CITY, UTAH, April 15 & May 1, 1900


A BEAUTIFUL LITTLE BOOK.

	A very attractive little book has recently
been issued from the Juvenile Instructor
office, entitled The Latter-day Prophet, a
history of Joseph Smith, written for the
young people. The book is a suitable size
for children, the simplicity of language
used will recommend it to the little folks.
The loftiest subjects are so simplified as to
be understood by the children; at the same
time they retain the thrilling testimony that
always accompanies the truth. It is really
a narrative full of wonderful manifestations
of the power of God, and His dealings with
His obedient children.
	The subjects are arranged in short chap-
ters, which will make it the more helpful
to the young readers, and more adaptable
to Sunday School work.
	As a people we really have much need of
this class of books, and it is gratifying to
know that a man of such splendid attain-
ments as President George Q. Cannon has
made a study of the Prophet's life for the
children of the Saints. Joseph Smith natur-
ally loved little children; this is apparent in
his history, and has been handed down to
us as one of his characteristics by those who
knew him well personally. The children
of the Saints should learn to love Joseph
the Prophet through being acquainted with
his life and character, as well as to rever-
ence him because of his greatness as a servant
of God, prophet, seer and revelator.
The book should be in every home where
there are children, and it should be studied
carefully. There are still a few people
living who knew Joseph Smith personally,
and who testify of his tenderness to every
living creature and his love for flowers and
music, poetry and the drama, also of his
fondness for athletic games. He was un-
doubtedly the most remarkable man of this
century, and the time will come when his
superiority will be widely acknowledged.
	President Cannon has given the story
of  the golden plates in the most happy style,
minutely describing their appearance, also
the golden breastplate and the Urim and
Thummim, the hill in which the stone box
was hidden and the various times the angel
Moroni appeared to him and conversed with
him about them. The terrible persecutions
the Prophet endured because of the power
of evil, and his great courage and fortitude
are forcibly portrayed.
	It is a fine book for the little men and


women, the best, as far as we have seen, of
its kind; but it has the best subject to deal
with and the writer knows how to reach the
hearts of his readers, and partly because in
this instance his own has been thrilled with
the story he relates.
	The book is for sale at George Q. Cannon
& Sons Co. Publishing House, Main St.,
Salt Lake City, Utah. Price 50cts.


[Column 1]
		PARIS EXPOSITION.

	Paris, April 14.— The Paris Exposition
was opened at noon April 14. The most
favorable weather conditions prevailed and
innumerable bright colored domes glistened
in the sunshine. Paris was early astir with 
people wending their way towards the
Elysee and in the direction of the exposi-
tion.
	All the public buildings and numberless
private houses were decorated with trophies
of flags, chiefly the tricolor. The neighbor-
hood of the exposition was especially gay
with bunting, while most of the pavilions
themselves were surmounted with floating
banners.
	President Loubet, as he stood in the
presidential tribunal surrounded by the
cabinet and household, opened the exposi-
tion with the words: "I declare the expo-
sition of 1900 open.”
	Thus was the Paris Exposition, designed
to celebrate the world's entry into the new
century, inaugurated, and shouts of “ Vive
Loubet” and ‘‘Vive la Republique' ’ rose
from his 40,000 hearers, and silk hats were
waved in the air. The crowds outside the
hall took up the cheering and the exposition
was open.
	The spectable inside the Salle de Fetes
was brilliant with color and illumination.
The immense circus shaped hall is decor-
ated with magnificent frescoes, represent-
ing allegorical subjects by famous French
artists and its stained glass cupola bears
superb designs by Hermann and Goodwin.
It formed a beautiful setting for todays
ceremony.
	Contrary to expectations, the premier, M.
Waldeck-Rousseau, made no address at the
opening of the exposition, Mm. Millerand
and Loubet being the only speakers.
	President Loubet saluted the committees,
and entered the hall through, the throng of
more than 40,000 people that formed a sea
of human faces all turned toward the door.
As a salvo of artillery announced the presi-
dent’s arrival, there was a moment of ex-
pectant silence, then the heavy red plush
cushions covering the door were thrown
aside and the president in evening dress and
wearing the broad red insignia of the Legion
of Honor across his breast stepped into the
tribune which had been temporarily erected
for today’s ceremony, the presidential gal-
lery which is to serve for all later cere-
monies not yet being ready. At the same
instant an orchestra composed of musicians
of the famed Republican Guard struck up
the Marsellaise. The whole assembly un-
covered and remained bare-headed during
the remainder of the proceedings.
	Before the strains of France’s stirring
national hymn had died away a mighty cry
arose of “Vive Loubet, vive la Republique,
vive la F[-]ance!”
	President Loubet stood in the front of
the tribune with M. Waldeck-Rousseau,
M. Deschanet, president of the chamber of
deputies, and M. Delcasse, minister of
foreign affairs and all of the ministers ex-
cept the Marquis de Gallifet, minister of

[olumn 2]
war, who was absent because of indisposi-
tion.
	Grouped behind him were a number of
richly uniformed officials. The acclama-
tions were still echoing through the al-
coves and galleries when President Loubet
and the ministers resumed their seats and
M. Millerand began his speech formally
handing over the exposition to the chief of
state.
	M. Millerand’s address was received with
rounds of applause which attained a veri-
table roar at its conclusion.
	President Loubet then arose and delivered
an address which was also cheered.
	M. Waldeck-Rosseau was up to the last
moment expected to speak, but he changed
his mind, and when President Loubet con-
cluded he remained seated. The orchestra
then rendered Saint Saen's hymn to Victor
Hugo, and President Loubet descended
from the trifiune. 
	Americans will especially be proud of
their country’s display at this world’s fair,
for the United Stales stands second only to
France herself in number of exhibitors,
which treble those of any foreign country.
	The highest testimonial of Americanhood
comes from Commissioner General Picard
himself. In comparing the state of pro-
gress of the installation of various nations,
he said to Commissioner Peck, ‘‘It is an
object lesson to us all to see the American
people’s work. I thank you for your
promptitude and the advanced condition of
work in the United States section.”


RELIEF SOCIETY CONFERENCE.

	The General Conference of the Relief
Society was held in the Salt Lake Assembly
Hall, in this City, Thursday, April 5,
1900, President Zina D. H. Young presid-
ing. Her counselors, Jane S. Richards,
Bathsheba W. Smith and Sarah Jane Can-
non, the secretary, E. B. Wells, and Presi-
dent M. Isabella Horne, of the Salt Lake
Stake of Zion, and many presidents from
other Stakes were on the stand.
	Meeting opened at 10 a. m. by singing
“Zion stands with hills surrounded.”
Prayer by B[-]other Redding Allred. Singing
duet by Sister Christine Anderson and her
daughter, Miss Judith Anderson, "Whisper-
ing Hope,” very beautfully rendered, Miss
Lizzie Thomas accompanying them upon
the organ.
	Secretary called the roll, response from
thirty-eignt Stakes, eighteen presidents, ten
secretaries, ten by other representatives.
Minutes of previous conference read and ac-
cepted.
	President Young gave an interesting ad-
dress of welcome and greeting to the sisters
assembled, and asked the Lord to bless us
all and renew our spirit and strength and
comfort us with His Holy Spirit.
	President Jane S. Richards would much
rather have heard from “Aunt Zina,” and
felt some others would, but wished to be
obedient at all times, and as she had been
called hoped she might be able to say a few
words of encouragement. “The Lord said
He would have a tried people and we want
to be His. Sometimes we would rather
choose our trials, but it is not given us to
do. If all had come to this meeting who
ought to have, there would not be a vacant
seat, that is how it should be. The society
was organized for the care of the sick and I


have never stayed at home when I felt that
I could go. Sister Zina wants the presi-
dents to visit their Stakes once a year at
least, if not more. We are so glad when
we can see the sisters. If we exercise faith
I believe Sister Zina will be able to visit the
sisters in their conferences.” Sister Rich-
ards urged the sisters to be good to poor
people, for we are all dependent upon the
same Father. If some are wayward, let us
coax them to return if we can. There are
many who need spiritual strength, and
there’s a great promise to the lost sheep,
we will make mistakes, but I don’t think
we should wait until people are dead, or
can’t hear us, let us do this and set a good
example.
	Sister B. W. Smith said: ‘‘My dear sis- 
ters, I greet you with a joyful greeting. I
remember when we first set out to comfort
the sick and afflicted, and to comfort each
other and look after the wayward. I was
asked to speak about the first organization
and how things were then. There were
only a few, nineteen in all, and the society
grew and increased to several hundreds and
a great interest was taken. We want to
teach righteousness in all things and to
comfort each other, and do whatever we
can to help build each other up.” Sister
Smith spoke of observing the 17th of March
and paying the donation. She would like
to see the sisters come into the Temple; how
would we feel when we pass away if we
have not done this. She recommended
reading the life of Joseph Smith in the
society, knew that he was a man of God; she
had been well acquainted with him enough
to know his daily walk and conversation.
Let us enjoy ourselves and never be weary
in well doing.
	President Young spoke upon prayer, com-
fort and wisdom, the great responsibilities 
of mothers, the foundation of all is prayer;
then urged all to pay their tithing and do-
nations, and encourage those who are not
interested to attend our meetings. It the
hill of corn is not fed by the elements it will
not grow. Let us teach the children indus-
try, how to save and economize. Referred
to Sister Eliza’s words about idleness and
uncleanliness; said she knew Sister Eliza
almost seventy years ago, and was thankful
for her being upon the earth; that we
knew her and had her with us. Loved to
look upon the faces of the Saints, they had
come from under the water, had received
the Holy Ghost. Let us do our duty and
our labors will be crowned with success
when the last day comes.
	Sister Horne: “When we hear the word
of the Lord given to us, we should observe
it. We’ve had many good things this
morning, and we should digest them when
we are performing our labors. We know
the Lord is as good as his promises, and we
should follow the teachings of the Savior
and acknowledge His hand in all things;
by this means we are proven. We are
called to help accomplish the work of God.
Solomon in summing up said: Fear God
and keep His commandments was the
whole duty of man. We have had so much
instruction from Sister Eliza and Mother
Whitney in the past, we should remember
them in love, and we expect to meet them
when we go behind the veil. There are
many insinuating spirits abroad, and we do
not want to be overcome by them. If we
walk humbly the Lord will arrest calamity
and when diseases come that baffle the
skill of the physician as mentioned in the
Book of Doctrine and Covenants, don’t be

[Column 2]
fearful, ask the Lord to help you, trust in
Him, and may the Lord help us to under-
stand our duty and teach those in our care
to keep the Sabbath day holy and work
righteousness.” Sister Horne closed with
a blessing upon all.
	Sister Mary J. West Counselor in the
Snowflake Stake, Arizona, gave an excel-
lent report of the Stake. Said they had
two Relief Society halls, they gave the first
one they built freely for the Academy and
then put up a beautiful little brick build-
ing. The spirit of the Lord is with the
Saints there, and they are comforted and
blest. Sister West offered a beautiful tri-
bute to the memory of Sister Lucy H.
Flake, who had recently passed away. She
had been president of the Primary Associa-
tions of the Stake. She was one of the
most useful women, a true Saint of God.
Sister West bore a strong testimony to the
divine mission of Joseph Smith and prayed
that the aged mothers in Israel might live
many years to teach the daughters of Zion.
	Sister Zina Y. Card, president of the Y.
L. M. I. A. of Alberta Stake, reported the
Relief Society of that Stake of Zion which
she designated as the baby Stake. The
society have one house which they pur-
chased. The 17th of March this year was
observed throughout the Stake in Cardston.
The society gave a banquet to the old
folks, all over fifty were invited. She
realized it was easier for the Saints to live
their religion in new settlements where all
were equal. Sister Card spoke strongly
for the support of the Exponent and for
the central building in this city, and alto-
gether her speech was replete with a living
testimony of the truth.
	President Elizabeth Fox, of Oneida
Stake, said she had not been able to attend
conference for a year and a half. Rejoiced
in being present and seeing the sisters who
had labored in the Relief Society ever since
the first organization. Referred to the
branch of the society in Oregon as belong-
ing to their Stake. Had tried to encourage
the sisters in taking the sacrament, knew it
was a blessing to the sisters in the society.
Prayed that we might increase in wisdom
and understanding, and train our youth in
the principles of the Gospel, teach them to
observe the law of tithing while they are
young, be charitable to the wayward and
they will turn back. Asked every blessing
upon the Saints in the name of Jesus, amen.
	Sister L. M. Hart, counselor in Bear
Lake Stake, made an excellent report. The
president, Sister Lindsay, was ill and could
not attend the conference. She referred to
the visit of Sister Jane S. Richards and
Apostle F. D. Richards to their Stake dur-
ing the last summer and how much they
appreciated the instructions particularly
upon tithing. In that Stake the society
was helping to build the Stake Academy.
On the 25th of December they made a party
for its benefit and it was the party of the
season; they realized $240.00. She felt a
school for the children of our own faith
would be the best influence for the boys
and girls to train them in the Gospel
principles. They were very much lacking
skilfull help in nursing. Physicians were
reaping a harvest. Referred to the teach-
ings of President Woodruff and felt to bear
them in mind.
	Congregation standing sang, “O, ye
mountains high’’ and benediction was offered
by President Jesse M. Smith.
E. B. Wells, Secretary.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT


A BRIEF SKETCH OF MY LIFE.
	My parents names were William
Cruickshank. and Mary Farquhar Cruick-
shank. I was born in Aberdeen, Scotland,
June 5, 1825, and lived there until my mar-
riage, which event took place December 22,
1843. My husband’s name was William.
Morrison, also a native of Aberdeen.
	In 1844 my husband received an appoint-
ment in Her Majesty’s dockyard, Woolwich,
Kent, England, and thither we went, in-
tending to spend the remainder of our lives
in happiness and comfort. But alas! we
are short-sighted mortals, for our Heavenly-
Father had ordered it otherwise, and dur-
ing the summer of 1847 my husband heard
the principles of the Gospel as taught by
the Latter-day Saints, which aroused in
him a spirit of inquiry to know more about
them. He had been longing for something
and knew not what, but the desire of his
heart was soon to be granted, he being
earnest and prayerful, and his prayers were
answered.
	At this time I went on a visit to my
native home and stayed four months. Dur-
ing my absence my husband had ample
opportunity to investigate for himself, and
soon became convinced of the truth of the
great Latter-day work, which soon proved a
source of joy and satisfaction to him.
	During my stay in Aberdeen my little son
was taken down with scarlet fever and
putrid sore throat; and although everything
was done that medical skill could suggest,
it was all to no purpose, my darling was
taken from me and removed to a higher
sphere and I was left to mourn.
	During this short period my husband had
heard the. Gospel preached by one of the
Elders of the Church, Thomas Bradshaw.
He believed and was baptized for the re-
mission of his sins and received the gift of
the Holy Ghost, which gave him light,
knowledge, wisdom and understanding, and
he was enabled to communicate with me
words of comfort and consolation in the
hour of our severe affliction, this being the
first great sorrow of our lives. But “The
Lord giveth and the Lord taketh away.
Blessed be his name.”
	Three weeks after the death of my boy,
the Lord blessed me with another son, and
was soon able to return to my husband
with a joyful heart, and with joy he received
me and preached to me the principles pf the
Gospel of repentance and baptism for the
remission of sins, which I gladly accepted,
making the bonds that united us more firm-
ly riveted than ever. I was baptized into
the Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day
Saints, January 1, 1848, by Elder Thomas
Bradshaw. I felt convinced of the truth
from the first I heard it, and the Lord has
blest me ever since. He enlightened my
mind and gave me a clear conception of the
principles of eternal truth, as they were ad-
vanced to us from time to time through
divine revelation, and I have truly rejoiced
from that day to the present.
	Then the desire to emigrate took posses-
sion of us from the very beginning, and we
were in earnest looking Zionward all the
time.
	In 1850 I had a daughter born, and again
three weeks later death robbed me of my
only boy. But we prayed to our Father in
heaven to give us strength to bear our
trouble, that we might be able to hold up
under every trial.
	In 1852 another daughter was born to us,
and three weeks later my dear sister, who


[Column 1]
with her husband had embraced the Gospel,
died in childbed, leaving her husband and
one son four years old, her babe being
buried with her. It really seemed as if our
troubles were greater than we could endure,
but with prayerful hearts, exercising pati-
ence and perseverance, the Lord helped us
and we were enabled to break up our home
and make a start for Zion. Thus in Sep-
tember, 1854, we bade farewell to father,
mother, brothers and sisters, and all we
held most dear on earth, to travel to Zion in
obedience to the commands of our Father in
heaven, trusting in Him and looking ahead
for a brighter day to come.
	Our passage from Liverpool to New
Orleans took eleven weeks, and from there
to St. Louis two weeks more, making our
journey by water thirteen weeks. When
we got to St. Louis they would not allow
us to land, so we had to be quarantined two
weeks longer, and during that time we took
the cholera, thirty of our company died. I
had it very bad, but was instantly healed 
by the power of God but my two little
girls and my sister’s boy all died in one
week.  Being left childless our trials were
almost more than we could endure, but the
Lord was with us and strengthened us for
the trying ordeals we had to pass through.
When we arrived at St. Louis we found our
means had all been taken to emigrate the
poor.  As we belonged to the independent
company we had to remain there two years
and make a new outfit, which we did, and
were again able to start in April, 1856.
	During our stay in St. Louis I had a
daughter born who lived just ten months;
so, you see, I left three of my own girls and
my nephew laid there to rest to await the
resurrection of the just. Surely I had all
I could endure, but the Lord gave me
strength equal to my day, and I never once
looked back.
	We arrived in Salt Lake City September
22, 1856, just three weeks before the first
handcart company. We got us a home in
Salt Lake City on our arrival, and were
very comfortable. We had a son born
December 9, 1856, all well.
	We remained in Salt Lake City until the
brethren were called to Echo canyon to
meet Johnston’s army, my husband being
among the first. During his absence
Bishop Woolley called from the stand to
find out who would be ready to start south
next morning. I raised my hand, and
started at 9 a. m. with my infant son,
Willie, in my arms. My friends came
around me and begged me not to go farther
than Provo and they would soon come.
They were Sisters Kent, Richards, Caine,
Gates, Colbrook.
	So we said goodbye for a little while and
traveled on till we arrived at Provo, and
the brother who owned the team said,
“Now, Sister Morrison, this is Provo, how
far will you go with us?’’ I said, “How
far do you go?’’ He said, “One hundred
miles farther.” “Then,” said I, “if you
will take me I will go as far as the team
will carry me, for I feel that is where the
Lord wants me to be.’’ So we drove right
to Ephraim. I did not see my husband for
eleven weeks, but it was a joyful greeting
when we did meet.
	We lived in Ephraim from the spring of
1858 till the spring of 1859, my daughter,
Mina being born on March 13. As soon
as I was able we moved to Mt. Pleasant,
where we remained living in the Fort [-]ll
the spring of 1865. February 15 my
daughter, Tina, was born, and one month

[Column 2]
later we moved into our new “Home sweet
Home.”
	In 1865 my husband was called upon to
lead a company of thirty, to make a loca-
tion in Sevier Co., which he did in 1867.
The settlement of people were driven from
their homes by Indians and lost all they
had. By this time my husband's families
were getting numerous, he having three
wives. After a while President Young
called on those Saints to try it again, which
they did, and have remained there ever
since.
	In 1868 Bishop Wm. S. Seeley was called
upon to organize the Relief Society, which
he did and nominated me as president. He
also nominated Sister Madsen first counselor,
Sister Dehlian second counselor. When
called to fill this important position I felt
helpless as a child, and felt it utterly im-
possible for me to fulfill the duties of that
office with propriety. I prayed earnestly
to the Lord to help me and give me wisdom
that I might be able to do my part in a man-
ner that would be pleasing and acceptable
unto Him, also to fill the office with credit
to myself and satisfaction to all concerned.
	It will be thirty-two years on the 11th of
May since our Relief Society was organized,
and I can truthfully say that our sisters
have been all that I could wish or desire.
They have been always ready and on hand
to give aid and assistance when called upon,
to visit the sick or to minister to the wants
and necessities of the poor and those in
distress. I can also say that no one has
ever called for help and been refused. With
regard to the dead of Mt. Pleasant, we sis-
ters have dressed and laid away between
six and seven hundred, and you know that
means work. But the Lord has greatly
blessed our labors and given us strength
equal to the task before us.
	I am proud to be able to say the sisters
are most liberal in contributing to the wants
of the poor, also in responding to any call
made upon them, and do so cheerfully,
without a murmur or complaint. I know
it is due to the prayers of the sisters who
are associated with me, that I have been
enabled to keep on as I have and feel as I
do. Our desire is to live humble and faith-
ful, and strive to do unto others as we
would wish to be done unto.
	That we may be able to run the race and
gain the prize which has been laid up for
the faithful, is my desire and prayer in the
name of Jesus, amen.
					M. F. C. MORRISON.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


PIONEER WOMAN.
With seventy-five years of life behind her
and another well nigh completed, and with
many a hardship bravely borne, Mrs.
Elizabeth Murphy McLelland, a pioneer of
’48 and widow of Thomas McLelland,
second Bishop of the Seventh Ward, of this
city, passed from mortality at her home in
Farmers Ward, Thursday, April 12.
The . deceased was born in Manahan,
Ireland, in Ausust, 1824. Having em-
braced the “Mormon” faith she sailed from
her native land in 1844, and went to Nau-
voo where she resided until the expulsion
of the Saints. During her residence there
she became well acquainted with the
Prophet Joseph Smith and was a firm be-
liever in him and the doctrines he taught.
Mrs. McLelland was among the first
“Mormons” who departed from Nauvoo,but
remained in the various camps in Iowa un-
til the fall of 1848 when she came on to


Utah one year after the arrival of her hus-
band’s team.
    Seven living children, numerous grand- 
children and a host of friends mourn Mother
McLelland’s demise. Her children are, in
the order of their ages: Mrs.Thomas A.
Starr, of Cassia County, Idaho, who arrived
in Salt Lake a few days before her mother’s
death; Mrs. Margaret Palmer; Thomas
McLelland, of Farmers Ward; Edward
McLelland, ex-deputy sheriff; Sarah Lisle,
of San Mateo, California, and Mrs. Horace
Stayner, of Salt Lake.

	ANNIVERSARY CELEBRATION.

    On March 17th last the Relief Society of
Forest Dale Ward convened for the purpose
of celebrating the first organization of the
Relief Society, Louisa Stevenson presiding.
    After singing and prayer, the minutes of
the first organization of the Relief Society
were read, after which the following pro-
gram was rendered:
    Sketch of the life of Sister Bathsheba W.
Smith, by Stake Counselor Mary P. Young.
Duet, by Miilie Kimball and Maggie
Summerhayes. Select reading, by Sister
Belle Hansen. Recitation, “The Changed
Cross,” by Sister Nellie Fowler. Song, by
Sisters Libbie Pratt and Maggie Summer-
hayes. Guitar selection, by Hazel and
Geneva Love. Song, by Hannah Gates
and Rhoda Laird.
    There was a good spirit manifest and all
rejoiced exceedingly in the work of the
Lord.
				AGNES RUMEL, Sec.

		ORANGEVILLE.

Relief Society was held in the Relief Soci-
ety hall, March 17, 1900, at ten o'clock a.
m., and was a testimony meeting, with
President Rhoda E. Robertson presiding.
Singing by the Relief Society choir, “How
firm a foundation.” Prayer by Charles H.
Oliphant. Singing, “Praise to the man who
communed with Jehovah.”

visitors who were with us. Reading from
the WOMAN'S EXPONENT, by Lucinda C.
Curtis.
    Stake President Anna U. Larson ex-
pressed her pleasure in meeting with the
Orangeville Relief Society. Said she had a
testimony to bear; she would never have
left her native land but for the knowledge
that this was the work of God.
    Maria Guymon bore her testimony that
this is the work of the Lord, and Rachel 
Killian felt proud to say that she had al-
ways been a member of the Relief Society.
Felt that mothers had one of the greatest
missions on earth to fill in rearing their
children properly.
    Sister Susanna Jewkes bore her testi-
mony to the goodness of the Lord. Bishop
Jasper Robertson said the spirit of the Lord
was with us, and that thousands were cele-
brating this day. President R. G. Miller
congratulated the sisters on the great oppor-
tunities they enjoy.
    Professor B. Cluff, Jr. bore a strong testi-
mony to the divine mission of the Prophet
Joseph Smith.
    Singing “O, say what is truth.”
    Professor George Cluff spoke of the many
branches of the Relief Society that exist to-
day, and all carrying peace and kindness
with them.
    Singing, “The spirit of God like a fire is


[Column 1]
burning,” Benediction by Bishop Robert-
son.
	Afternoon session, presided over by Sister
Jane Moffatt. Singing, “Now let us re-

Singing, “O, my Father.” Opening re-
marks by Rhoda E. Robertson. Song by

gave a sketch of the first Relief Society or-

di[-]on by A. G. Jewkes.

KAMAS.
	The sisters of the Kamas Relief Society
met in annual anniversary meeting at the
Kamas meeting house, March 17, 1900,
President Ann Harder presiding. A num-
ber of the brethren were also present.
	The choir sang “An angel from on high.”
Prayer by Sister Laura Pack. Singing,
“Praise to the man.”
	President Ann Harder made a few pleas-
ing opening remarks, adding that as Sister
Ruth Pack was unable to be present on ac-
count of illness, Sister Julia I. Pack would
give an address.
	Sister Pack expressed pleasure at the
privilege of celebrating this anniversary,
spoke of the first organization of which she
was a member, and of the wonderful increase
and progress made, and of the great amount
of good accomplished. Prayed the Lord’s
blessing on all faithful workers in the
cause.
	Presidents May Simpson and Katie
Burbidge sang a duet.
	Sister Emma Pack recited “The Starless
Crown,” after which the minutes of the
first organization were read by Counselor
Margaret A. Woodard, who added a few
interesting remarks on the work of the
society.
	Counselor Willet S. Harder spoke on
Church organization and Relief Society
work and bore a powerful testimony.
	President Ward E. Pack spoke of the
great mission of the sisters and their power
for good, and of the aid rendered in this
Stake in assisting the authorities in accom-
plishing good.
	President Ann Harder gave a short sketch
of work done the past year, and made all
present welcome to the feast.
	An original poem was read by Sister
Edith Lambert, the choir sang “The Spirit
of God,” and closing prayer by W. S.
Harder.
	Tables were then set and all partook of
the good things prepared in rich abundance.
After social chat and reminiscences of other
days, the company separated with the
feeling that another pleasant landmark was
reached in the journey of life.
	EDITH LAMBERT, Ass’t. Sec’y.
RELIEF SOCIETY, PANGUITCH STAKE.
Reorganized.
	Our Relief Society was to be reorganized
at our Stake conference, and then the sis-
ter who was elected for president was absent-

[Column 2]
and we thought best to wait until she ac’
cepted and was set apart with the other
officers, which was done on the 17th of
March, at the close of our celebration, and
was as follows: Elizabeth Delong, presi-
dent; Alice Jane Cameron, Hannah A.
Crosby, counselors; Hannah A. Crosby,
secretary, M. Isabella Henrie, treasurer.
	Our president desired to attend the con-
ference and intended to if possible, and may
have been there, I am not quite sure. We
have had no Stake organization for nearly
two years and no report, except that we
have heard from all the wards and all are
in fairly good condition but one, Cleveland,
which is practically disorganized on account
of so many moving away.
	We feel that Sister Delong will fill the
position satisfactorily to all, and we will be
pleased to receive any instructions the
General Board may have to give.
	HANNAH A. CROSBY. Sec.
FARMINGTON
	It is some time since anything from our
Relief Society appeared in the columns of
the EXPONENT, we therefore thought per-
haps a brief report of our meeting of the
17th of March might be of interest.
	The meeting was held at the Farming-
ton vestry, President Helen Miller presid-
ing. After the Opening exercises the
secretary read an article from the Expo-
nent explaining the object of the meet-
ing, it being to celebrate the first organiza-
tion of the Relief Society by the Prophet
Joseph Smith.
	Mrs. Lucy A. Clark read the original
minutes of the first meeting held, with
Eliza R. Snow secretary, also gave a
sketch of the Relief Society of Farmington
from its organization, June 3, 1868, until
the present. It was very interesting.
	“O, my Father” was then rendered.
Sister Annie Tanner gave a much appre-
ciated sketch of the life and labors of Sis-
ter Eliza R. Snow Smith, and concluded
by giving one of her beautiful poems.
	Interesting and instructive remarks were
made, testimonies borne by Stake Counsel-
or Mary S. Clark, President Helen Miller,
Sisters Hannah Miller Julia Steed, Caroline
Bigler, Temperance Moon, et al.
	A very enjoyable time was spent, the
spirit of the Lord was with us in a marked
degree, softening the feelings of all pres-
ent so that many were in tears. After
singing and benediction meeting was ad-
journed.
	MINERVA RICHARDS KNOWLTON,
								Secretary.
TWENTY-FOURTH WARD ANNIVERSARY.
	Minutes of the entertainment given on
the 17th of March in honor of the first or-
ganization in the Twenty-Fourth Ward,
Salt Lake City. We had a very enjoyable
time and a good spirit prevailed.
	We commenced the meeting by singing
“We thank Thee, O God, for a prophet.”
Prayer by Brother Wilson. Continued by
singing “How firm a foundation.”
	Sister Mary Howell read the minutes of
the first organization in Nauvoo, Ill.
	Brother Gill made a few remarks. Said
he was pleased to be with us and spoke of
Relief Society work.
	Solo, by Lottie Cottom, “Flee as a Bird.”
	Recitation by Brother Cottom, Sen.,
“The Convert’s Dream,” composed when
Brother Cottom was imprisoned for his re-
ligious principles. Remarks by J. W. Roe,
who spoke about the celebration of the or-
ganization.

[Column 3]
	Brother Edwards sang a Welsh song,
“Time Waits for no Man.”
	Duet by Lunie and May Harmen.
	Refreshments were served by Sister Kate
Ames and her aids, and after intermission
the exercises were resumed.
	Song by Sister Johnson. Brother J. W.
Christenson made a few remarks and gave
good instructions to the society.
	President Nightingale thanked the sisters
for all they had done expressed her good
wishes and prayed God to bless us one and
all.
	Meeting closed by singing “Guide, us, O
Thou Great Jehovah.” Minutes of the
meeting were read and accepted.” Bene-
diction by Brother Backman.
		NELLIE A. MORRIS, Sec.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


OBITUARY.
Resolutions of respect to the memory of Sister
Josephine Hansen,
	Sister Josephine Hansen departed this life at her
home in Heber City, Utah, March 16, 1900, aged
fifty-nine years lacking one hour. Her death
happening at this time is a very sad occurrance, as
her husband, Peter Hansen, is laboring in the
missionary field in Denmark. He has been ab-
sent from home almost two years. She has been a .
faithful member of the Heber east ward Relief
Society.
	Resolved, That it is but a just tribute to the
memory of the departed to say that in sorrowing
over her removal from our midst, we mourn for 
one that was faithful and worthy of our respect
and love.
	Resolved, That we, the officers, in behalf of the
members of the east ward Relief Society, do
mourn for her, realizing that we have lost a noble
sister and colaborer.
	Resolved, That we tender our heartfelt sym-
pathy to the bereaved family.
	Resolved, That a copy of these resolutions be
placed on the records of the Relief Society and a
copy be sent to the WOMAN’S EXPONENT for publi-
cation.
				SARAH BOND, President.
				EDDA R. JOHNSON, Sec.
SISTER HENRIETTA HOMAR YOUNG.
	Sister Henrietta Homar Young was born in
Reisoer, Norway, April 6, 1842, was baptized into
the Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints in
1853 and was married to H. O. Young in Rockport,
Summit Co., Utah, November 12, 1860, and died
of heart failure, at Parley’s Park, Summit Co.,
Utah, October 27, 1899.
	She was a true Saint, a loving wife and mother
and was the mother of nine children, three of
whom died in infancy. She leaves a loving hus-
band, six children and a wide circle of friends to
mourn her loss. Her children are all married.
	She was an earnest worker in the Relief Society
and a true mother in Israel. Ten minutes before
she died she was engaged in helping with a party
for the first misssonary that had been called from
our ward. She died without a minute’s warning
to those around her. She was greatly beloved by
all who knew her and her death has cast a gloom
over all the ward. She was a kind, true friend in
joy or sorrow, and the only comfort there is in her
loss is knowing she has gone to the presence of our
Father in heaven.					COM.


PRIMARY CONFERENCE. 
	Minutes of Primary Conference of the
Salt Lake Stake, held in the Assembly
Hall, March 17, 1900, President Camilla
C. Cobb presiding. Opened by singing,
“We are the children of the Saints.” Pray-
er by Master David W. Cummings, of the
Nineteenth ward. Singing, “Did you
think to pray?” Roll call with twenty-
two associations represented, and minutes
of previous conference read and approved.
	The first exercise on the program was a
song of welcome, by members from the
Ninth Ward. Tillie Thomas, from the
Nineteenth Ward, read an essay On Pri-
mary work. The story of Samuel was re-
peated by Heber Mathews, from the Fifth
Ward. Motion song, by nine little girls


from the Eleventh Ward. Violin solo, by
Samuel Newton, from the Nineteenth
Ward. Questions and answers on the life
of Joseph Smith, by two little girls from
the Twenty-first Ward. Duet, by two
little girls from the Seventeenth Ward.
Mandolin solo, by Ettie Browning, from
the Third Ward.
	Counselor Joseph E. Taylor addressed
the conference. Expressed great pleasure
in listening to the exercises by the children.
Gave good counsel and advice, especially
to the boys in regard to swearing and being
destructive. Said children should honor
and obey their parents and teachers, their
love and counsel cannot be exceled. Asked
the children why we were brothers and
sisters? Said it was because we were
baptized into the Church of Jesus Christ.
Spoke words of praise for teachers and
children. Prayed God to bless us.
	President Nellie C. Taylor, of the Y. L.
M. I. A., complimented the children on
their good behavior and urged them to be
prayerful. Related an instance of a little
boy coasting, who came very nearly being
killed, and knew he was saved because he
had said his prayers that morning.
	A duet was rendered by Silva and
Frances Vowley, from the First Ward.
	President Louie B. Felt, of the General
Board, said she had listened with pleasure
to the sentiments that had been given; was
also pleased with the large attendance of
children. Urged them to remember what
Brother Taylor had said to them in regard
to obeying parents and teachers. Wanted
every little boy and girl to be a missionary
and bring some one with them to the next
conference.
	By request the children from the Eleventh
Ward gave another motion song.
	President Zina D. H. Young, of the
Relief Society, spoke a few words to the
children in regard to having faith to be
healed; made other remarks.
	Singing, “We thank Thee, O God, for a
prophet.” Benediction by Master Eli
Eliason, of the Thirteenth Ward.
			ELLA W. HYDE, Sec.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


U. W. P. C.
	The U. W. P. C. held its regular
meeting in the EXPONENT office, on March
21, 1900, the president, Mrs. Schiller, pre-
siding. Opening prayer, Ruth M. Fox.
The roll was called, members responding
with original sentiments, after which the
business before the club was considered.
	The second circular on the Industral
Problem was read, and the president re-
ported from the club council. The Con-
sumers League was then discussed. A
communication was read regarding the edu-
cational work.
	Mrs. Wells announced to the club that
Mrs. Charlotte Perkins Stetson would be
here on the 25th of April. A recommend-
ation was read asking for the names of the
club, after which the following program
was carried out:
	A poem composed by Lucy A. Clark,
entitled “Wedding Bells,” was read by
Ruth M. Fox. Phebe C. Young read a
paper on Manilla, and Aimee C. Schiller
read Current Events.
	It was decided that the club hold a special
meeting during the month, invite the
Reapers’ Club to meet with us and have
Emma J. McVicker talk on education. 
	Prayer, Rebecca Doolan.
				GRACE ANDERSON, Sec.


LETTER TO SILK RAISERS.
	Mrs. Margaret A. Caine, secretary of the
Utah Silk Commission, has issued a circu-
lar letter to prospective silk culturists, a
copy of which here follows:
	“The Utah Silk Commission desires to
call attention to the fact that the time for
raising silk is again upon us, and we trust
that those who intend entering upon the
work this year will write to us at once for
eggs. Those who are taking up the work
for the first time will kindly state the num-
ber and size of the trees from which they
can obtain leaves; also the number of per-
sons who can give their time during the
latter period of the raising season, so that
we will be able to send the necessary quan-
tity of eggs.
	“We also desire to state that eggs can be
obtained from the commission free, and we
would prefer every one who is going to
raise the worms to have seed from us, as
we provide a quality which gives a much
better result than those raised by people
having no experience. We will also send
pamphlet of instructions free to those wish-
ing any information upon the subject.
	“We trust that many people throughout
the State have already planted numbers of
mulberry trees, and have been watchful
lest any of those which have grown are in
danger of being destroyed. We also wish
to say to those who have trees, and do not
care to use them, that they may do a great
deal towards establishing this important
industry by allowing others to use the
leaves, and we assure you that it will only
benefit the trees even if they are stripped
several times, they will soon become much
more thrifty, and this will also be a means
of disposing of the fruit which is sometimes
an annoyance, especially when trees are
planted on the street.
	“As a whole, the commission feels very
much encouraged with the results of last
years work, and trust it will be very much
increased this year. All communications
should be addressed to Margaret A. Caine,
Secretary Utah Silk Commission, Salt Lake
City, Utah.
